### I-Law Net-Benefit\-\--Shell 

#### [Consultation in the Arctic revitalizes international law by resolving contradictions. Thumpers don't assume to magnitude of the internal link.]{.underline} 

Sara **Olsvig 24**, University of Greenland, Nuuk, Greenland, Miriam
**Cullen**, Associate Professor, Faculty of Law, University of
Copenhagen, Copenhagen, Denmark, 03/26/2024, "Arctic Indigenous Peoples
and International Law"

https://tinyurl.com/yshzzpa9, eb

[Even aspects of [international law designed]{.mark} to [protect]{.mark}
the rights of [Indigenous Peoples]{.mark} can have the paradoxical
[consequence]{.mark}]{.underline} of [[facilitating]{.mark} greater
[exclusion]{.mark}]{.underline}. For instance, that states have an
[[obligation]{.mark} to obtain Indigenous Peoples' free, [prior and
informed consent]{.mark} in decisions which affect them is [not]{.mark}
fully [implemented]{.mark}]{.underline} in Greenland. The governments of
both Greenland and Denmark agree that for the purposes of ensuring
adherence to the principle of obtaining Indigenous Peoples free, prior
and informed consent, the consent of the Greenlandic Government alone is
sufficient.37 This [interpretation is not in keeping with obligations
under the UN Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples or
Convention No. 169 on Indigenous and Tribal Peoples in Independent
Countries, nor the broader suite of human rights obligations under both
International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (iccpr) and
International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural
Rights]{.underline} (icescr).

The [consultation and participation of Indigenous Peoples in
decisions]{.underline} [which affect them is not
satisfied automatically merely because the Government is made up of
people who identify as Inuit.38 Such an interpretation undermines the
purpose of the principle which]{.underline} is to [prevent harm to those
affected by the relevant decision]{.underline}, and compromises the idea
that Indigenous Peoples "engage in decision-making processes through
representatives chosen by themselves and in accordance with their own
procedures ...".39 Although today the Government of Greenland is elected
by a populace who are predominantly Inuit, that may not always be the
case. Most Danish citizens who have held residence in Greenland for six
months can stand for election and vote. In addition, political leaders
must take into account the interests of a range of other stakeholders,
including their non-indigenous constituents, business interests, other
interest groups, as well as internal stakeholders in exercising
legislative and executive authority.40 That a political executive could
claim to be the exclusive voice of Indigenous Peoples could lead to a
paradoxical result in which the purported implementation of
participatory rights effectively diminishes, rather than advances
them,41 for instance if the demography of Greenland shifts, or the
Government of Greenland prioritises other considerations over Inuit
rights. That is not to say that the Government of Greenland could never
fulfil such a role, rather, it cannot be assumed that it does so as a
matter of default.42

[Arctic [Indigenous]{.mark} Peoples are [experiencing new
encroachments]{.mark} on their rights in the name of environmental
protection]{.underline}, reminiscent of historical dispossessions and
the long-standing misalignment between the interrelated rights of
Indigenous Peoples and the desires of those claiming to safeguard or
represent the interests of nature and the environment. The term "green
colonialism" has been used by Sámi in particular, and was also referred
to in the Joint Statement of the 2023 Arctic Peoples' Conference to
describe "land encroachment, resource extraction, renewable energy
production, and protectionist conservation ... undertaken at the expense
of Indigenous Peoples' reality".43 When pursued in this way, the green
transition reproduces colonial logics and can lead to actual or
effective dispossession of Indigenous Peoples' natural resources,
territory, and culture. Moreover, it infringes the distinct rights held
by Indigenous Peoples including self-determination, free determination
of political, social, economic, and cultural development, and free,
prior, and informed consent in decisions that affect them.44

For example, the Øyfjellet wind energy project in Norway is located in
the middle of an important reindeer migration route and pasture area.
The project first broke ground in April 2020 after a fiercely contested
licensing process.45 The construction and presence of the subsequent
windmills has compromised indigenous knowledge and Sámi capacity to
continue traditional herding practices. Yet some litigious action has
succeeded in halting such developments. In 2021, the Supreme Court of
Norway found that the Norwegian Ministry of Petroleum and Energy's
decision to grant a license for wind energy infrastructure on the Fosen
Peninsula was a violation of reindeer herders' right to enjoy their own
culture under Article 27 of the iccpr. The decision echoed earlier
concerns of the UN Special Rapporteur on the Rights of Indigenous
Peoples that the installation of wind power plants in territory
traditionally used by Sámi could constitute an unlawful limitation of
Sámi rights.46 The court's reasoning stood in contrast to a much earlier
decision of the Norwegian Supreme Court (1982) which upheld a government
decision to build hydroelectricity infrastructure on the Alta River,
construction for which was completed in 1987.47 Thus, while tensions
between Sámi rights and green energy infrastructure projects are not
themselves new, the success of arguments grounded in international law
is a more contemporary development.48

Inuit are also increasingly approached with suggestions for projects
that would fundamentally alter their way of life in the name of the
climate response, although Inuit have not (yet) resorted to litigation
to respond. Scientists rush to engineer mechanisms to slow the melting
of the Greenland ice sheet, but often do so with little or no input from
the Peoples on whose territories the relevant infrastructure will be
placed. Such technologies are often profoundly invasive because they
require large-scale manipulation. The Polar Umbrella for instance, would
utilise massive canopies to cool the surface of the icecap by deflecting
sun and each would be so large as to host helipads and tourist
attractions. Underlying such initiatives is a prevailing and problematic
assumption that the interests of Indigenous Peoples
are inherently aligned with the mechanisms for implementing the green
transition. To be sure, Arctic Indigenous Peoples have long been
supportive of measures to diminish the harms caused by climate change
and to ensure sustainable approaches to natural resource
management.49 However, they will resist initiatives that do so at the
expense of their inherent rights, existence or way of life, or in
contravention of the norms of international law they have long fought to
secure (as the Joint Statement of the 2023 Arctic Peoples' Conference
attests).

While new in its tenacity and clarity, the 2023 Joint Statement also
reflects some longer-held positions of Arctic Indigenous Peoples. In
2010, for example, after years of preparation and revisions, the Inuit
Circumpolar Council issued a single Inuit Arctic Policy, which
prioritised the recognition of the interests of Inuit as a single Arctic
People living across four very different nation-states. The policy is
distinct from those issued by states, which typically view the Arctic as
a site of resource extraction, and potential transit, with the rights
and interests of Arctic Indigenous Peoples routinely ignored, or
mentioned superficially, which is inconsistent with Indigenous Peoples
rights to directly participate in decisions which impact them.

[Climate change continues to constitute an existential
threat]{.underline} to [Arctic Indigenous Peoples]{.underline} and their
livelihoods, and unjust green transition and climate mitigation
initiatives [add to existing pressures]{.underline}. This is not aided
by geopolitical developments in and around the Arctic. The Arctic has
been the object of great-power military competition for centuries, and
Arctic Indigenous Peoples have long navigated icy political waters
across state boundaries, not least during the militarization of Inuit
territories during and between the first and second World Wars. A 1977
Inuit Circumpolar Council resolution concerning peaceful and safe uses
of the Arctic Circumpolar Zone exemplifies longstanding Inuit insistence
on maintaining a peaceful region.50 More recent military threats have
triggered renewed recognition of the strategic advantage of the Arctic.
Here too, actions based on real or perceived military imperatives add to
other forms of new colonialism, in which neo-colonial arguments
insistent on "greater good" imperatives, ignore Indigenous Peoples'
knowledge, interests, and sovereignty and, more fundamentally their
rights, under international law entirely.

Traditional [security concerns]{.underline} have also [undermined some
of the advances by Arctic Indigenous Peoples]{.underline} in regional
arrangements such as the Arctic Council. Established in 1996, the
purpose of the Arctic Council is to provide a means for promoting
cooperation, coordination and interaction among Arctic states, with the
full participation of Arctic Indigenous Peoples.51 It is made up of
eight member states and six organizations representing Arctic Indigenous
Peoples'. [The Arctic Council is unique because it has always made
decisions based on consensus, which grants its Indigenous participants
significant informal power.]{.underline} Moreover, given the regularly
changing governments within the state apparatus, the [Indigenous
participants make up the Arctic Council's institutional
memory]{.underline}. [When Russia invaded Ukraine]{.underline} in 2022,
seven of the eight Arctic states paused the Arctic Council's
proceedings, and there was some concern that the [[Arctic
Council]{.mark} might [no longer function]{.mark}]{.underline}, which
would eschew Arctic Indigenous Peoples' meaningful participation in
regional governance. The [[Inuit]{.mark} Circumpolar [Council]{.mark}
and the [Saami Council]{.mark}]{.underline} each represent peoples on
the Russian side of their homelands too, and are
[[accustomed]{.underline}]{.mark} [to [navigating]{.mark} tense
[East-West cooperation]{.mark}]{.underline}. The situation was partly
resolved in May 2023 when Arctic Council, in accordance with its rules
of procedure, held its biennial meeting and facilitated the transfer of
its chairmanship from Russia to Norway.52 It nevertheless illustrates
the importance of regional arrangements for [Arctic Indigenous
[Peoples']{.mark} inclusion in processes which affect them, their value
when they are included, and at the same time their [continued
susceptibility]{.mark} to the [unilateral decision-making of
states]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Although, Nordic states have stood at the forefront of international
advocacy for the rights of Indigenous Peoples in multilateral fora,
their commitment to the implementation of those norms abroad often
contrasts with their implementation of them at home. Arctic Indigenous
Peoples have sought to address the shortcomings in human rights
implementation through their own self-governance arrangements and
associated ngo s. It is challenging work, particularly when undertaken
in the context of a lack of adequate resources, the cultural domination
of settler states, and when it must be performed by people who are
already spread thin across multiple roles and legal systems. For Inuit
and Sámi, regular re-configuration to fit the remit of a state-based
system of international law is both par for the course and immensely
challenging. For instance, in relation to enhanced participation [in the
UN system, Inuit are yet to determine which institution(s) constitute a
representative body for them]{.underline}. Nor do Inuit have a
collaborative parliamentary body across the national territorial
boundaries akin to the Sámi Parliamentary Council, and neither Sámi nor
Inuit can currently engage fully with their Russian counterparts. The
recognition of Indigenous Peoples' interconnected and indivisible rights
have been achieved in the UN Declaration. [However, the nation-state
model of the [existing world order]{.mark} must continue to
[evolve]{.mark} progressively, [in line]{.mark} with [Indigenous]{.mark}
Peoples' direct, full and [effective]{.mark} [participation across the
Arctic]{.mark}.]{.underline}

4 Conclusion

The [state-based system of [international law]{.mark} is replete with
[examples]{.mark} of its [structured
contradictions]{.mark}]{.underline}.53 [It [forbids]{.mark} the
initiation of [war and]{.mark} also [provides]{.mark} the [rules for its
conduct]{.mark}, [facilitates]{.mark} international industrial [trade
and the exploitation]{.mark} of non-renewable resources, [and]{.mark}
also [purports to limit emissions]{.mark}, and in this case, it provides
rules to uphold Indigenous Peoples' rights while at the same time paying
homage to the wealth and sovereignties constructed at their expense.
[International law underpins]{.mark} the capitalist-[colonial
project,]{.mark} while at the same time [attempting to minimise the
damage]{.mark} of that very endeavour. The international legal system is
comprised of states, but the world is made up of peoples, who live as
nations across, within, and between, state borders.]{.underline}

[[Despite various failures]{.mark} of [international law]{.mark} to
recognise or address centuries of structural violence,
dispossession]{.underline}, and [disenfranchisement]{.underline},
[[Arctic Indigenous]{.mark} Peoples]{.underline} have, through their own
persistence, [mastered and [contributed]{.mark} to the
[development]{.mark} of the Western European]{.underline} [[legal
norms]{.underline}]{.mark} in a system that once [facilitated their
colonisation]{.underline}. The fiftieth anniversary of the Arctic
Peoples' Conference was a moment to reflect on these and other
achievements of Arctic Indigenous Peoples, building upon the 1973
resolutions and addressing contemporary challenges. The 2023 Joint
Statement addresses the need for enhanced engagement in the forums of
international law, intergenerational justice, intersecting rights to
wellbeing, land, water and natural resources, and to address the risks
posed by climate change and colonialism. It reflects on Arctic
Indigenous Peoples' "contributions to various national and international
governing bodies and how our way forward was paved by the people, now
elders, who first came together to envision a better
future."54 [Although Arctic Indigenous Peoples "have survived, and
thrived, through hundreds of years of colonization ... many challenges
and colonial systems remain to be dismantled]{.underline}."55 W[ithin
the forums of international law, Arctic Indigenous Peoples continue to
work to address inequalities in representation and recognition, and to
insist that the [rules-based international legal]{.mark} order
[upholds]{.mark} the [rights, interests, and sovereignties]{.mark} of
those it has systematically excluded.]{.underline}

#### International Law solves [global cooperation]{.underline} and a list of [existential threats]{.underline}. 

Alina **Habba 24**, 2-21-2024, \"The Role of International Law in a
Globalized World\", Medium,
https://alinahabba.medium.com/the-role-of-international-law-in-a-globalized-world-2cf5a89e8292

In today's [[interconnected]{.mark} and rapidly evolving [global
landscape]{.mark}]{.underline}, the [[importance]{.mark} of
[international law cannot be overstated]{.mark}]{.underline}. As the
world becomes increasingly [interconnected through trade, technology,
and communication]{.underline}, the need for a framework of rules and
regulations to govern international relations becomes paramount. In this
article, we will explore the [role of international law in a globalized
world and examine its significance in shaping the behavior of states,
[promoting cooperation]{.mark}, and [addressing global
challenges]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Establishing a [Framework for Cooperation]{.underline}: International
law serves as a framework for cooperation among states, providing a set
of rules and norms that govern their interactions in various domains,
including diplomacy, trade, and security. [By establishing clear
guidelines and standards of behavior, international law helps to prevent
conflicts and disputes between states and promotes peaceful
resolution]{.underline} of disputes through negotiation and diplomacy.

Promoting Peace and Security: One of the primary objectives of
[international law is to [promote peace and security]{.mark} in the
global community]{.underline}. Through instruments such as [treaties,
conventions, and agreements, states [commit]{.mark} to [upholding
principles of sovereignty, territorial integrity]{.mark}, and
[non-aggression]{.mark}]{.underline}. International law also
[[regulates]{.mark} the [use of force]{.mark} and provides
mechanisms]{.underline} for [conflict]{.underline} [prevention,
management, and resolution]{.underline}, such as the United Nations
Security Council and international peacekeeping operations.

Facilitating [Economic Development and Trade]{.underline}: International
law plays a crucial role in [[facilitating]{.mark} [economic
development]{.mark}]{.underline} and trade by providing a framework of
[rules and regulations that [govern international
commerce]{.mark}]{.underline}. Treaties and agreements, such as free
trade agreements and investment treaties, establish rights and
obligations for states and create a [predictable and stable environment
for businesses to operate across borders]{.underline}. International law
also addresses issues such as [intellectual property rights, competition
policy, and environmental standards, ensuring fair and equitable
treatment for all parties involved.]{.underline}

Protecting Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms: Human rights law is an
essential component of international law, providing a framework for the
protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms on a global scale.
International human rights instruments, such as the Universal
Declaration of Human Rights and the International Covenant on Civil and
Political Rights, establish universal standards for the protection of
individuals' rights to life, liberty, and security of person, as well as
freedoms of expression, assembly, and religion.

Addressing Transnational Challenges: In an increasingly interconnected
world, transnational challenges such as climate change, terrorism, and
pandemics require coordinated and collective responses from the
international community. International law provides a framework for
addressing these challenges through mechanisms such as multilateral
treaties, conventions, and agreements. By establishing rules and norms
for cooperation and coordination, international law enables states to
work together to tackle shared problems and achieve common goals.

[[Upholding]{.mark} the [Rule of Law]{.mark}]{.underline}: The rule of
law is a [[fundamental principle]{.mark} of [i]{.mark}nternational
[r]{.mark}elations]{.underline}, ensuring that states abide by their
legal [obligations and commitments under international law]{.underline}.
International legal institutions, such as the International Court of
Justice and international tribunals, play a crucial role in interpreting
and enforcing international law and holding states accountable for their
actions. By upholding the rule of law, international law promotes
stability, predictability, and fairness in the global community.

Balancing Sovereignty and Global Governance: International law seeks to
strike a balance between state sovereignty and the need for global
governance in addressing common challenges and promoting common
interests. While respecting states' sovereign rights and
responsibilities, international law also recognizes the
interconnectedness of states and the importance of collective action in
addressing global issues. Through mechanisms such as international
organizations, treaties, and conventions, international law provides a
framework for collective decision-making and cooperation among states.

Adapting to Evolving Realities: As the global landscape continues to
evolve, [international law must [adapt]{.mark} to [new realities]{.mark}
and [emerging challenges]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Rapid
[advancements]{.mark} in [technology]{.mark}]{.underline}, shifting
geopolitical dynamics, and global crises such as climate change and
pandemics require innovative approaches and flexible mechanisms for
international cooperation. International law must remain responsive and
adaptive to these changes, ensuring its relevance and effectiveness in
addressing contemporary global issues.

In a globalized world characterized by interconnectedness and
interdependence, international law plays a crucial role in shaping the
[behavior of states, promoting cooperation, and addressing global
challenges]{.underline}. By providing a framework for cooperation,
[[promoting peace and security]{.mark}, [facilitating]{.mark} economic
[development]{.mark} and trade, protecting human rights, addressing
transnational challenges]{.underline}, upholding the rule of law,
balancing sovereignty and global governance, and adapting to evolving
realities, international law serves as a cornerstone of the
international system, guiding states' interactions and promoting the
common good of humanity. As the world continues to evolve, the
importance of international law in promoting stability, justice, and
cooperation on a global scale will only continue to grow.

[Under the influence of positivist thinking from Vattel and Marshall's
[court decisions]{.mark}, international law [shifted]{.mark} to
[reject]{.mark} any "[consideration]{.mark} of Indigenous peoples as
political bodies with [rights]{.mark} under international

first three premises are that "[[international law]{.underline}]{.mark}
is [[concerned]{.mark} [only]{.mark} with the
[rights]{.mark}]{.underline} and [[duties]{.underline}]{.mark} of
states," [that it upholds the "exclusive sovereignty of

Indigenous peoples do not fall under the "limited universe" of states
that make and are polities to international law. 51 The primary deciding
factor in why Indigenous peoples were excluded from being in this
"family of nations," was because other states did not consent to it---in
other words, the only way to become a nation under international law was
to be recognized as a state by others nation-states who are already in
the 'family.'52 [This concept moved beyond the earlier theories about
justification of conquest through war, and simplified the [legal
legitimation of colonization]{.mark} by providing a legal
mechanism---approval through the 'family of nations'---by which to
justify conquest and [claims to sovereignty]{.mark} over [Indigenous
peoples]{.mark} [and territories]{.mark}]{.underline}.53 Thus, these
positivist approaches to international law systematically advantaged
nation-states and this framing formed the foundation for the use of
state sovereignty as a conceptual backing in the oppression of
Indigenous peoples54---an argument that remains relevant today.

There were a [number of important international tribunal
decisions]{.underline} in this time period that [demonstrated the
dominance of positivist thinking]{.underline} and the exclusion of
Indigenous rights in international law. The first was a 1926
international arbitration tribunal ruling that stated that Great
Britain's claim for the "[Cayuga Nation" was not legal]{.underline}, yet
they could make claim to the Cayuga Indians living in Canada because
they were British nationals.55 The [decision [asserted]{.mark} that
Indian tribes were "[not a legal unit of international
law]{.mark}]{.underline}."56 In 1928, an international arbitration
between the United States and the Netherlands ruled in favor of the
Netherlands claim to the Island of Palmas because of its "effective
occupation and display of authority on the island," compared to the US's
inherited claim to prior discovery through Spain.57 Through this
decision, the tribunal ruled that the treaties the Dutch had made with
the Indigenous peoples of the island were merely 'facts' relevant to the
case, but that these types of contracts (ie. treaties with groups not
recognized in the family of states under positivist international law)
were not "capable of creating rights and obligations" "in an
international law sense."58 The third case--- which occurred in 1933 and
is of particular relevance to this paper---was regarding the legal
status of Eastern Greenland. [The Permanent Court of International
Justice ruled that while it recognized the Inuit population in Eastern
Greenland, the territory's legal status was nonetheless defined by
sovereignty claims from the Denmark and Norway]{.underline}.59 This
decision effectively [ignored Inuit claims to territorial
sovereignty]{.underline} or Inuit efforts to block and disrupt Norwegian
and Dutch occupation and colonization, an exemplary illustration of the
positivist hierarchy that placed nation-state sovereignty over

Part I reviews how treaties were historically relatively flexible
bilateral agreements concluded against a stable background of default
customary international law. While foundational customary norms and
bilateral agreements are still the norm, today\'s international legal
structure is complicated by a proliferation in multilateral treaties and
an increasing demand for international regulation of new areas,
technologies, objects, actions, and ideas. In the absence of directly
relevant treaty law, and in need of reliable guiding principles, states
are developing practices standardizing their rights and duties in these
new spheres. As a result, treaty text is increasingly running up against
conflicting state action and swiftly developing customary international
law. Part II describes consent-based means of modifying treaties and
concludes that these traditional methods do not legitimately resolve all
conflicts between treaty and later-in-time customary international law.
Part III demonstrates that the possibility of treaty modification by
customary international law has long existed in the international legal
structure and evaluates different doctrinal justifications for such
modification. Part IV employs the threatened U.S. unilateral use of
force in Syria as a case study to tease out the relative benefits and
drawbacks of these different means of treaty modification.

I. A New International Legal Order

Customary international law and treaty law are the two primary sources
of international legal obligations. Sometimes they operate
independently, governing particular fields; sometimes they serve as
mutually reinforcing regulations; sometimes one fills the other\'s
lacunae; sometimes they mandate apparently contradictory actions. As
this Article is concerned with how customary international may modify
treaties, this Part traces how, due to ideological, geopolitical, and
technological developments, the relationship between these two sources
of international legal obligations has grown more complicated and more
prone to conflict.

\[\*242\]

A. The Classic Account

1\. Stable Customary International Law

A rule of customary international law is recognized as existing when
states generally engage in specific actions (the \"state practice\"
requirement) and accept that those actions are obligatory or permitted
(the \"opinio juris sive necessitatis\" element). Thus, unlike custom in
many domestic legal systems, which derives much of its authority from
its long-standing nature, customary international law has no formal
temporal requirement. Instead, a rule of customary international law is
authoritative because states generally abide by it in the belief that it
is law.

That being said, because of the generalized state practice requirement,
customary international law was slow to develop in a world of limited
communication and sporadic technological advances. Accordingly, historic
customary international law comprised long-established, well-known, and
relatively fixed rules governing relations among all states. It
regulated the recognition of new states and state responsibilities; the
exchange of diplomatic counsels and their immunities; the conduct and
resolution of wars; the creation, interpretation, and termination of
treaties; and other subjects associated with state interaction.

2\. Flexible Treaties

Against this background of static customary norms, states concluded
\[\*243\] bilateral treaties - written documents memorializing
agreements between two states - clarifying their respective legal rights
and duties. These treaties were relatively flexible legal regimes: they
could be modified or terminated with the consent of states parties, by
the conclusion of a subsequent, conflicting treaty between the same
parties, by the denunciation of one party after a material breach by the
other, or by a fundamental change in circumstances or other supervening
event resulting in the impossibility of performing a promised legal
obligation. Additionally, certain types of treaties - for example,
commercial or trading treaties or treaties of alliance - were generally
presumed to allow for unilateral denunciation. Accordingly, treaties
have long been celebrated as a source of adaptive positive law that
reflects states parties\' needs.

B. The Modern World

[[Today\'s international]{.mark} legal [structure is]{.mark} far more
[complicated]{.mark}]{.underline}. Certain customary rules still serve

1\. Constitutive Treaties

The past century has seen a dramatic rise in multilateral treaties -
treaties \[\*244\] concluded by multiple countries that often aspire to
universal participation. As early as the 1920s, scholars were
recognizing that there were \"an increasing number of multilateral
treaties.\" According to one study, eighty-six multilateral treaties
were concluded in the century between 1648 through 1748 - but more than
two thousand such treaties were concluded in the twenty-five years
between 1951 and 1975! This [proliferation might be traced to a growing
conviction that certain [global problems]{.mark} - including

#### The counterplan [solves]{.underline} the aff and sets a [positive precedent]{.underline} for future consultation\-\--that [cements]{.underline} tribal sovereignty and improves climate change [impact assessments]{.underline}.

**Warner et al. 21** \[Elizabeth Kronk Warner is the Jefferson B. & Rita
E. Fordham Presidential Dean at the S.J. Quinney College of Law at the
University of Utah, as well as a Professor of Law. Kathy Lynn is a
faculty researcher in the University of Oregon's Environmental Studies

Consultation", *The Federal Lawyer*, pp 59-60.
https://dc.law.utah.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1001&context=tcproject\]
吳

[[Positive examples of]{.mark} effective [consultation]{.mark} between
tribes and the federal government [prove instructive]{.mark} as to what
effective consultation can look like]{.underline} and demonstrates how
parties can incorporate the principles articulated in the ethics and
morality literature. For example, the importance of the
government-to-government relationship is emphasized in the 1994
Northwest Forest Plan (NWFP), which addresses management of federal
forest land in the Pacific Northwest within the range of the northern
spotted owl.37 The Record of Decision (ROD) for the NWFP recognizes that
the implementation of the NWFP may affect tribal treaty rights and trust
resources, as restrictions under the NWFP may limit access to tribal
cultural resources; calls for consultation on a government-to-government
basis with tribal governments when treaty-protected lands or trust
resources may be affected.38

Agencies managing federal land within the NWFP region are required to
monitor the effects of implementation and evaluate the conditions and
trends of trust resources identified in treaties with tribes as well as
protections for, access to, and use of forest species, resources, and
places that are in religious and cultural heritage sites.39 These
[monitoring reports have consistently found that, [while consultation is
recognized in fed]{.mark}eral [law]{.mark}]{.underline} and
administrative policy as the primary mechanism for federal agencies to
work with tribes when federal action may impact tribal lands and
resources, [consultation does not always ensure that tribal interests
are upheld]{.underline}. In fact, [[consultation
may]{.underline}]{.mark} in some cases [[be little more than
notification]{.mark} of planned federal action]{.underline}.40 [[This is
evi]{.mark}dence [of]{.mark} what this article concluded
earlier]{.underline}---that although federal law may require
consultation in some areas, [[little guidance]{.mark} is given as to
what effective consultation looks like.]{.underline}

\<\<CONDENSED, NONE OMITTED\>\>

The NWFP requires a series of monitoring reports to be conducted every
five years to assess a broad spectrum of issues, including populations
and habitat of the northern spotted owl and marbled murrelet,
late-successional and old growth forests, watershed conditions,
socio-economic conditions, and the tribal-federal relationship. As an
initial starting point, it is laudable that the NWFP seeks to
re-evaluate the tribal-federal relationship. This is consistent with the
idea expressed in the ethics and morality literature that relationships
should be dynamic partnerships. Since 1999, the USDA Forest Service
Regional Ecosystem Office has published these monitoring reports that
document the status and trends of these issues over time. The most
recent Tribal Monitoring Reports (for the 15-year, 20- year, and
forthcoming 25-year reports) have followed a protocol developed by the
NWFP Tribal Monitoring Advisory Group to examine consultation processes,
the affect of the NWFP on tribal values of interest (including cultural,
social, and economic resources), and strategies to strengthen
federal-tribal relations. To accomplish this, the monitoring team has
reached out to tribal council members and tribal staff from all of the
75 federally recognized tribes with tribal lands and/or territories
within the NWFP boundary in Washington, Oregon, and California in order
to assess the impacts of the NWFP on tribes. Approximately 1/3 of the
tribes within the NWFP region have participated in each of the past
three monitoring reports.41 Pursuant to the interview protocol
established by the Tribal Monitoring Advisory Group, the recommendations
in the monitoring reports have focused on consultation, tribal rights,
and access to cultural resources and improving the compatibility of
federal-tribal forest management practices. In all of the tribal
monitoring reports, many of the respondents focused on the need for more
effective consultation that would move agency practices from merely
notifying tribes of proposed actions to engaging tribes to work with
federal agencies to develop strategies that would meet tribal cultural
resource management objectives. Recommendations to strengthen
consultation focused on increasing agency accountability for meeting the
federal trust responsibility through staff education and training;
developing formal agreements for consultation and
government-to-government interactions, such as memorandums of
understandings; and ensuring that agency and tribal leadership
understand and come to agreement about consultation policies and
practices.42 The NWFP tribal monitoring reports have also examined the
extent to which tribal rights and access to cultural resources have been
impacted by the NWFP. The 20-year tribal monitoring report describes
some of the ways that tribal rights and access to resources have been
impacted by the NWFP, including "road closure, decreased ability to
harvest traditional cultural resources, reduced economic opportunities,
and limitations on land management."43 Recommendations to improve tribal
rights and access to cultural resources under the NWFP focus on training
agency staff across all levels to ensure strong cultural competency in
tribal matters, reviewing and updating policies that severely impact
tribes' rights to interact with traditional lands and resources, and
adopting practices that protect sensitive tribal and traditional
knowledge. The NWFP monitoring reports also look at federal-tribal
forest management compatibility. Interviews that took place for the
20-year tribal monitoring report described some ways that federal forest
management practices align with tribal values, restoration and
protection of fish and wildlife habitat, and the incorporation of tribal
forest management practices in agency land management (e.g., prescribed
fire). Some of the ways that respondents described incompatibilities in
tribal and federal forest management included prioritization of timber
and industry over other forest resources and tribal needs, lack of
incorporation of traditional knowledge and tribal values into
management, an all-or-nothing approach that could deplete ecosystems or
impact economies.44 Recommendations to improve the compatibility of
federal-tribal forest management focus on increasing formal consultation
and collaborative approaches between federal agencies and tribes to
enhance the compatibility of federal-tribal forest management practices.
This would increase opportunities for tribal leadership in land
management decisions and leverage opportunities for funding and
resources to support tribal natural resource departments. A 2018
synthesis of science to inform land management within the NWFP area
examined strategies to promote tribal ecocultural resource management
and effectively engage tribes in forest management and planning.
Ensuring effective consultation was among the recommendations included
in the report, along with strategies for bolstering federal-tribal
collaboration, coordination, and cooperative management of tribally
valued cultural resources.45 The NWFP, with its five-year review cycle
and constant reflection on what constitutes effective consultation
tribes with area tribes, demonstrates the principles for effective
consultation articulated in the ethics and morality and Indigenous
studies literature. This is because the ideal of consent, as a moral
norm, suggests a relationship between the United States, tribes, and
other parties that establishes collaborative processes and partnerships
as mechanisms to help achieve more effective consultation. Conclusion:
Strengthening Federal-Tribal Relationships to Address Climate Change and
Fossil Fuel Industries

combine to diminish the strength of the pump that keeps the
[AMOC]{.mark} circulating]{.underline}. [Such changes have additional
operational implications given that they [impact]{.mark} the propagation
of sound waves in underwater acoustics, which are of particular
importance to [anti-submarine warfare]{.mark}]{.underline}.25
Year-round, in situ data collection is necessary for monitoring changes
as they evolve. While an abrupt change of state in the AMOC is viewed as
very unlikely by the IPCC, it would have profound impacts were it to
happen, so should be included in low-probability, high-impact
assessments.

[The AMOC and other [Arctic]{.mark}-linked climate [tipping points are
linked to tipping points elsewhere]{.mark} in the world, [such as the
Amazon]{.mark}'s status as a rainforest versus becoming a
savanna]{.underline}. [There is risk that [if one]{.mark} critical
climate tipping point's threshold [were]{.mark} to be [toppled it could
create a domino effect]{.mark} that impacts stability of the entire
climate system]{.underline}.26

Changing strategies and new tasks

[The Russian Arctic contains several climate change
hotspots]{.underline}, such as the sea areas around Novaya Zemlya, which
have been warming "up to seven times as fast as the global average."27
[Russia's northern flank is becoming more exposed [as sea ice]{.mark}
retreats, and Russia's remilitarization of its Arctic zone]{.underline}
-- in the years prior to their cataclysmic decision to wage war against
Ukraine in February 2022 -- [was partially a response to this increased
threat perception]{.underline}. These strategic sites have both
defensive and offensive capabilities, and in the new security
environment have taken on greater significance. At the heart of Russia's
bastion defence concept -- and at the core of the Arctic's strategic
significance to Russia -- is the Kola Peninsula, which houses Russia's
strategic submarines.

[Russia has significant strategic military infrastructure in the
Arctic]{.underline}, most if not all of which will be exposed to one or
more climate hazards. Given that the extent of risks to infrastructure
are only just beginning to be understood and changes are accelerating,
[[this may heighten the Kremlin's]{.underline}]{.mark} --- and
particularly President Vladimir Putin's --- [perception of
[vulnerability]{.mark}, which experts have [linked to]{.mark} a higher
likelihood of Russia resorting to "nonconventional tools, including
[cyberattacks]{.mark}, [disinfo]{.mark}rmation campaigns, [covert
op]{.mark}eration[s and nuclear weapons]{.mark}]{.underline}."28 Indeed,
[Russia's Arctic policy]{.underline} through 2035 [identifies the
inability of the existing Arctic environmental monitoring network to
adequately respond to environmental challenges as a significant
threat]{.underline}.29

### Climate (Green Technology)\-\--2NC 

**Solves better than the aff and prevents the perpetuation of green
colonialism**

**Bushman 25** \[Victoria Qutuuq; conservation biologist, the
International Arctic Research Center, University of Alaska Fairbanks and
the Greenland Institute of Natural Resources, pHD in Conservation
Biology; Arctic World Wildlife Fund; "Indigenous Peoples have the
knowledge and practices to support climate resilience,"
https://www.arcticwwf.org/the-circle/stories/indigenous-peoples-have-the-knowledge-and-practices-to-support-climate-resilience/\]
//AChiong

[[Indigenous communities]{.mark} in the Arctic]{.underline} have
something to say about climate change---namely, that with the proper
support in policy- and decision-making, they [[are
resilient]{.mark}.]{.underline} Many [[Indigenous hunters]{.mark},
fishers, herders, harvesters and other knowledge holders have [provided
countless records of climate change's]{.mark} disruption to the
stability of snow, ice, water, ecosystem dynamics and wildlife
populations]{.underline}. Now, VICTORIA QUTUUQ BUSCHMAN explains, [the
world is beginning to recognize that Indigenous communities [have
considerable strength and adaptive capacity to provide solutions]{.mark}
to the impacts of climate change.]{.underline}

On February 28, 2022, the global community received the new
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) report on the impacts,
adaptation and vulnerabilities of ecosystems, biodiversity and people
amid climate change. In part, the report explains what's at stake and
how we're attempting to address these issues.

But this isn't the first report to make a strong call for the support of
Indigenous communities and practices. The 2019 [IPCC Special Report on
Climate Change and Land recognized that [Indigenous communities actively
safeguard]{.mark} the [biodiversity]{.mark} within their
homelands.]{.underline} The 2022 report reiterates what Arctic
Indigenous communities have always known: [that we [have the knowledge
and practices to identify]{.mark} impacts, [evaluate]{.mark} risks [and
adapt to climatic changes as long as we prioritize **self-determination,
Indigenous rights and inclusive governance.**]{.mark}]{.underline}

Recognizing the legacy of colonialism

Indigenous authors---including Dr. Tero Mustonen, head of Snowchange
Cooperative in Finland, and Dr. Dalee Sambo Dorough, chair of the Inuit
Circumpolar Council---led a body of experts across the circumpolar North
in developing the report's assessment of the impacts of climate change
on Indigenous Peoples and the implications for adaptation and governance
in the Arctic. The report recognizes that the legacy of colonialism is
behind Arctic Indigenous Peoples' climate vulnerability. Land
dispossession and displacement, the introduction and proliferation of
carbon-intensive economies, discrimination, racism, marginalization, and
social, cultural and health inequities all limit Indigenous Peoples'
abilities to respond to impacts effectively.

These impacts include human health risks and injuries related to
unpredictable and unsafe environmental conditions on land, water and
ice. They also include food insecurity and the instability of harvested
food sources, which are affected by changes in seasons and preparation
conditions as well as shifts in the sizes and structures of populations.

In addition, waterborne diseases have emerged, and pathogens such as
anthrax and smallpox have the potential to resurface in buried carcasses
as permafrost thaws. Additional impacts include mental health problems
related to climate change and heightened by colonial contexts, the
stress of livelihoods and culture continually upended by climate
impacts, and the inflexibility of policies and laws that were not
designed to accommodate rapid environmental change.

[Resilience linked to self-determination]{.underline}

Despite these challenges, Indigenous communities display inherent
climate resilience---a quality that is important for adapting to climate
change. There is growing recognition that this [[resilience is linked
to]{.mark} Indigenous [self-determination]{.mark}]{.underline}. The IPCC
calls for equity by recognizing that Indigenous communities' ability to
adapt to climate change is linked to this self-determination in
adaptation strategies, governance and ecosystem-based policies. It also
acknowledges that [[developing]{.mark} local definitions of social and
economic success and culturally legitimate government and [leadership
is]{.mark} a [significant]{.mark} part of this process.]{.underline}

[Recognizing Indigenous knowledge as a valuable and valid way of
knowing]{.underline} is another. The document calls for [the use of
Indigenous knowledge in planning, decision-making and management
processes, and in implementing adaptation.]{.underline}

[The Arctic provides ecosystem services for Indigenous
communities]{.underline}. In turn, [[Indigenous]{.mark} knowledge can
detect change, evaluate risk and inform adaptation. I]{.underline}t also
provides cultural ecological services. Despite barriers to the use of
knowledge in institutions and international fora---and within research,
management and implementation processes---Indigenous knowledge-based
solutions and inclusive ecosystem-based policies have shown the ability
to [[address climate change]{.mark} impacts.]{.underline} To continue to
do so moving forward, we need to create or [[improve
decision-making]{.mark} processes and adaptive management in human
[health,]{.mark} [Arctic shipping]{.mark}, tourism, reindeer herding,
commercial [fisheries,]{.mark} marine and terrestrial [subsistence, and
other areas]{.mark} of Indigenous concerns.]{.underline}

New tools needed

[This shift may [require new research, management, monitoring,
assessment, policy and governance structures]{.mark} that are equipped
to address the needs of Arctic Indigenous Peoples.]{.underline}

For example, research and climate modelling that focus on Indigenous
objectives are useful to communities planning to tackle issues such as
shoreline erosion, permafrost thaw, flooding, and changing wildlife
populations and food web dynamics. [[Developing green
technologies]{.mark} in partnership [with Indigenous Peoples]{.mark} can
[ensure]{.mark} that green [energy does not continue as a colonial,
externally imposed]{.mark} and inequitable [burden]{.mark} placed on
those already impacted and marginalized amid climate
change.]{.underline} Removing the external pressures that limit
autonomous evaluation, self-assessment and the response to climate
change---such as the often inflexible and centralized policies imposed
by state governments---can also support community-led adaptation.

[In the absence of national and international adaptation strategies in
the Arctic, [Indigenous communities are moving]{.mark} forward [with
adaptations at smaller scales]{.mark}.]{.underline} These contributions
may seem small, but they are being seen and heard around the world.
[[Supporting]{.mark} Indigenous communities' ability to make the
necessary [decisions]{.mark} and changes [will ensure a more resilient,
secure and equitable future for Indigenous]{.mark} Peoples [and]{.mark}
a healthier planet for [all.]{.mark}]{.underline}

### Climate (Traditional Knowledge)\-\--2NC

**Solves a litany of environmental risks**

**Ijatuyi and Lamm 25** \[Enioluwa Jonathan; Alexa; Kowiyou Yessoufou;
Terence Suinyuy; Hosea Olayiwola Patrick; August; African Centre for DNA
Barcoding, Department of Botany and Plant Biotechnology, PhD
Agricultural Extension; Department of Agricultural Leadership, Education
& Communication, Professor at University of Georgia, PhD Agricultural
Education and Communication; African Centre for DNA Barcoding,
Department of Botany and Plant Biotechnology, PhD in Botany;
Postdoctoral Research Fellow in chemical ecology and evolutionary
biology, professor at University of Mpumalanga; Postdoctoral fellow in
the School of Built Environment and Development Studies, Research Fellow
of the Earth System Governance Project; Environmental Science Policy;
"Integration of indigenous knowledge with scientific knowledge: A
systematic review," https://doi.org/10.1016/j.envsci.2025.104119\]
//AChiong

1. Introduction

1.1. Indigenous knowledge vs. scientific knowledge: a brief overview

[[Indigenous knowledge]{.underline}]{.mark} (IK) is deeply rooted in the
cultural and environmental contexts of Indigenous peoples,
[[covering]{.mark} land use, [ecosystems, medicinal plants, sustainable
ag]{.mark}riculture, and traditional healing practices which
cumulatively covers [environmental conservation,]{.mark} agriculture,
[and healthcare]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Whyte,
2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib150), [Jessen
et al.,
2022](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib60)).
For context, IK refers to the long-standing traditions, and intimate
knowledge of local ecosystems, biodiversity, and social systems
developed by indigenous peoples and local communities over time
([Berkes,
2009](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib16)).
IK, often perceived as informal, [is increasingly recognized as [a
sophisticated and dynamic system]{.mark} of understanding that is
[rooted in]{.mark} systematic [observation,]{.mark} empirical
[experimentation, and adaptive practices]{.mark}]{.underline}. Contrary
to the outdated view that positions IK as unscientific, researchers have
demonstrated that many indigenous practices are [[based on centuries of
trial-and-error]{.mark}, hypothesis testing, [and]{.mark} environmental
[monitoring]{.mark}]{.underline} [(]{.mark}[Berkes,
2012](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib17)).
For instance, traditional agricultural systems, such as those of the
Andean and African highlands, utilize complex soil and crop rotation
techniques that are not only ecologically sustainable but also tailored
to local microclimates---evidence of experimental and iterative
knowledge development ([Altieri,
2018](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib8)).

IK is a knowledge that is primarily preserved in the minds of
individuals and communities and is not physically documented ([Basdew et
al.,
2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib14), [Sraku-Lartey,
2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib132)).
It is often passed down orally from generation to generation and is
closely connected to the worldviews, values, and spirituality of
indigenous communities ([Agrawal,
1995](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib2)).
It is disseminated through various routes, such as children and elders
reciting stories, social, religious, or agricultural organizations
collecting records in the form of drawings, photographs, or sculptures,
and direct observation of the environment ([Basdew et al.,
2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib14), [Macchi
et al.,
2008](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib73)).
While IK is a critical part of society ([Sraku-Lartey,
2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib132))
that is passed down through generations, its magnitude decreases as
elders die and children move to cities and towns ([Elia et al.,
2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib39)).

[IK has been used for a long time, with evidence from the 1970s
suggesting it could [increase disaster preparedness]{.mark}]{.underline}
([Dekens,
2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib33)).
However, practitioners have only recently started to incorporate such
materials in policymaking and planning ([McAdoo et al.,
2009](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib79)).
[IK has been [instrumental in]{.mark} [helping]{.mark} rural subsistence
farming [groups adapt to climatic change]{.mark} and make daily
agricultural decisions, serving as [a means of survival for
millennia]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Lebel,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib71), [Graaff
et al.,
2009](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib46)).

Despite the efficacy of the indigenous knowledge systems (IKS), the
increase in the emphasis on a structured and systematic approach to
understanding the natural world through observation, experimentation,
and analysis as embodied in [the scientific knowledge (SK) system seems
to erode the wisdom and legitimacy of IK]{.underline} ([Goduka,
2012](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib45), [Ramirez
et al.,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib111)).
The relationship between IK and SK illustrates a [[vibrant exchange of
coexistence]{.mark} and opposition between the two systems of
knowledge.]{.underline} These two systems of knowledge embody unique
epistemologies, each with its strengths, methods, and outlooks on the
world[. Their coexistence [offers chances for mutual
enhancement]{.mark}, but resistance frequently arises from contrasting
views on how knowledge is validated, power dynamics, and the importance
of cultural preservation]{.underline} ([Rist and Dahdouh-Guebas,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib114)[).
Both IK and SK aim to comprehend and tackle issues like environmental
sustainability, resource management, and health.]{.underline} Although
they employ different methods, their [[common dedication to finding
solutions]{.mark} to similar community/environmental challenges [lays
the foundation for cooperation]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Priyadarshini and
Abhilash,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib107)).

IK channels play a vital role in [promoting adaptation and adjustments
in agricultural practices]{.underline} by providing valuable insights
into the communities, their efforts, and their agricultural situations
([Ifejika Speranza et al.,
2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib58), [Rosenthal,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib115)[).
[Supporting]{.mark} these [indigenous channels]{.mark} enables
researchers and financial organizations to access crucial information,
thereby [foster]{.mark}ing community [development]{.mark}]{.underline}
([Rosenthal,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib115)).
However, despite its importance, [[IK is not]{.mark} always [given the
recognition it deserves.]{.mark}]{.underline}

IK represents a culturally specific approach to agriculture that varies
across different cultures ([Zuma-Netshiukhwi et al.,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib157)).
It includes daily projections and seasonal forecasting, with indigenous
data guiding the timing of planting and harvesting ([Radeny et al.,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib109)).
[[Scientific advancements]{.mark}, as noted by ]{.underline}[[Raymond et
al.
(2010)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib113),
have [contributed to increased crop yields]{.mark} and [effective pest
and disease management]{.mark}.]{.underline} These advancements are
often documented and acknowledged as theories and principles ([Laudan,
1978](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib70)).
Moreover, scientific progress has [[addressed many global issues,
including climate change]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Oerke,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib98)).

Rural areas often lack access to modern technology ([Zomers,
2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib155)),
making it difficult for them to learn and apply modern and efficient
adaptation and mitigative practices ([Zuma-Netshiukhwi et al.,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib157)).
Scientific weather data is often difficult to understand for non-expert
community members, making decision-making difficult ([Zuma-Netshiukhwi
et al.,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib157)).
By combining indigenous and scientific information, rural farmers can
improve their agricultural operations holistically ([DeWalt,
1994](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib34)).

[Raymond et al.
(2010)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib113) [discuss
the significant challenges associated with integrating knowledge
systems, [emphasizing the need for]{.mark} the successful
[incorporation]{.mark} of information [to ensure authenticity and
validation]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Nah et al.
(2004)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib90) underscore
the importance of thoroughly examining the integration process,
techniques, and outputs to maximize the benefits for end-users. While it
is crucial for SK to supplement existing IK and (vice-versa) and enhance
decision-making in fields such as disaster management, agriculture,
healthcare, and environmental conservation ([Lebel,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib71)),
some critics argue that the integration process often faces practical
and ethical obstacles ([Shizha,
2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib124)).
One criticism is that SK may overshadow IK, reducing the latter\'s
perceived value and undermining the cultural and historical significance
of indigenous practices ([Rist and Dahdouh-Guebas,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib114)).
Additionally, the integration process can be complicated by differing
epistemological approaches and power dynamics between the two knowledge
systems[. It is essential to [ensure that IK]{.mark} is not merely
validated by scientific methods, but that [its unique contributions are
recognized and respected]{.mark} in their own right. [Without careful
consideration,]{.mark} the integration process [risks
reinforcing]{.mark} existing [inequalities]{.mark} and marginalizing
indigenous communities.]{.underline}

[The integration of IK and SK has been [of interest to]{.mark}
researchers, [policymakers]{.mark}, and practitioners in areas such as
environmental management, climate change adaptation, agriculture, and
healthcare]{.underline} ([Gadgil et al.,
1993](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib43); [Brondízio
et al.,
2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib24), [Rist
and Dahdouh-Guebas,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib114)).
The use of IK in conjunction with SK is increasing in popularity
([Stevenson,
1996](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib133), [McNie,
2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib84)),
[[but there is no formal framework to]{.mark} illustrate how these two
can reduce community vulnerability issues such as disaster and general
environmental impacts]{.underline} ([Zulfadrim et al.,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib156)).
Integrating IK and SK [[offers]{.mark} a robust opportunity to address
modern challenges and [leverage]{.mark}s [the strengths of both
knowledge systems]{.mark}.]{.underline} IK provides centuries of
observational expertise and interaction with local environments, while
SK utilizes systematic methods to analyse and quantify ecological data.
Together, [these perspectives lead to more comprehensive and effective
conservation and management strategies, enhancing our ability to
sustainably interact with and protect natural ecosystems.]{.underline}

This research focuses on the integration of IK and SK to address
contemporary global challenges, specifically environmental conservation,
agriculture, and healthcare[. The review uses literature to demonstrate
the strengths and limitations of integrating these knowledge
systems,]{.underline} aiming to provide a more holistic and effective
approach. The review also indicates insights for theoretical
contributions to the existing knowledge system.

1.2. Conceptual framework for the integration of indigenous and
scientific knowledge: insights for theoretical contributions to the
existing knowledge system

The integration of IK and SK provides valuable theoretical insights that
enhance our understanding of knowledge systems ([Chapman and Schott,
2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib28)[).
This integration [fosters the development of cooperative knowledge
system]{.mark}s that transcend disciplinary boundaries and [promote
intercultural communication]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Slikkerveer,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib127)).
By incorporating both indigenous and scientific frameworks into theories
of information exchange and collaboration, we gain a deeper
understanding of complex issues and potential solutions ([Mecer et al.,
2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib85)).
This approach [calls for more interdisciplinary and cross-cultural
methods of knowledge generation, challenging the compartmentalized
structure of academic fields]{.underline}. These insights question
traditional, compartmentalized views of knowledge and offer more
dynamic, inclusive, and sophisticated frameworks for understanding the
production, validation, and use of knowledge ([Langdon,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib68)).

The integration of IK and SK also leads to the creation of new ethical
frameworks for intellectual property and knowledge exchange
([Slikkerveer,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib127)[).
It [challenges the historically exploitative relationships between
Indigenous]{.mark} communities [and external researchers,
emphasizing]{.mark} the importance of [equity and respect]{.mark} in the
use of IK]{.underline} ([Orozco and Poonamallee,
2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib103)).
This integration advocates for a fair and equitable system where
indigenous groups retain control over their knowledge and benefit from
its use in global contexts ([Gebru,
2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib44)).
For example, ethical considerations in using traditional medicinal
knowledge for drug research highlight the importance of consent, fair
compensation, and cultural sensitivity ([Timmermans,
2003](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib140)).

Research by [Zulfadrim et al.
(2019)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib156) indicates
that IK evolves and is typically transmitted from one generation to the
next through oral histories, myths, songs, and tales. IK can be
categorized based on how it is acquired and transmitted ([Dekens,
2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib33)),
its aspects (traditions, religions, beliefs, culture), and local
knowledge (coping mechanisms over time). It can also be classified based
on practices, including common knowledge from everyday activities and
specialist knowledge held by selected community members and elders.

[Mecer et al.
(2010)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib85) propose
that [merging IK and SK can lead to [more effective risk
mitigation,]{.mark} bridging the gap between these knowledge systems.
Integrating both systems [requires a **shared understanding** of each
system\'s cultural and material foundations]{.mark}]{.underline}
[]{.mark}([Zulfadrim et al.,
2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib156), [Agrawal,
1995](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib2)).
IK is culture-specific and distinct from SK, which is based on
scientific research conducted in research institutions. SK systems are
part of society and contribute to humanity\'s scientific and technical
progress ([Hodson,
2003](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib54)).

Science and IK are interconnected, and their issues cannot be addressed
separately ([Ravetz,
2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib112)).
For example, [IK provides abundant agricultural knowledge and management
methods (]{.underline}[[Brush,
2005](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib25)).
While the use of synthetic fertilizers and large-scale pesticide inputs
in [industrial agriculture]{.mark} has led to numerous
problems]{.underline} ([Bhuyan et al.,
2023](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib20)), [Sarkar
et al.
(2020)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib120) suggest
that traditional agricultural wastes can be transformed into sustainable
and organic renewable inputs, such as plant sprays made from cow urine,
yoghurt, milk, and butter, which can replace synthetic pesticides and
foliar sprays with negative environmental impacts.

[Water is a limited natural resource that must be managed
wisely]{.underline}. At the Third World Water Forum in Kyoto, Japan
(2003), [Indigenous participants expressed their views on [water
management]{.mark} in the Indigenous Peoples' Kyoto Water
Declaration]{.underline} ([McGregor,
2012](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib81)).
Since then, it has been advocated that global water governance
frameworks align with the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples ([Taylor et al.,
2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib135)).
IK should be considered in water management decisions ([McGregor,
2012](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib81)).
[Various [IK systems have developed technologies for water conservation,
storage, and beneficial treatment]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Olokesusi,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib100)).
[For example, India\'s water tank systems are [part of the natural
hydrological cycle]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Lal,
2000](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib66)).
Many indigenous practices have been adopted by SK, which has claimed
originality ([Agrawal,
2002](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib3)).
Knowledge is passed down through generations and issues are solved
through practice, based on people-nature interactions ([Agrawal,
2002](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib3), [Diaw,
2005](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib35)).

[[When vaccines were not]{.mark} readily [available]{.mark} for an
ailment (global or local spread), [communities used]{.mark} their [IK to
create herbal remedies]{.mark} from various plants and
herbs]{.underline} ([Sadiku et al.,
2022](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib118)).
Although not approved by the World Health Organization as standard
solutions, [[countries like Madagascar and China]{.mark} still use IK
practices]{.underline} ([Basch,
1999](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib13), [Kugedera
et al.,
2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib65)).
[[A vaccine was developed through the integration of SK and
IK.]{.underline}]{.mark} With technology, these plants and mixtures can
now be packaged and [[marketed globally]{.mark} as ointments,
antibiotics, pain relievers, and other pharmaceutical
products]{.underline} ([Omotayo et al.,
2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib101)).
Given the current state of human civilization, many basic community
needs remain unmet, particularly in rural areas and overcrowded urban
centers. Investigating IK systems and SK can address various issues
effectively.

**Empirics prove**

3.3. Case studies of integration

[Several [case studies demonstrate]{.mark} how [integrating IK and
SK]{.mark} might [improve environmental
conservation]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} A national Inuit
organization [[in Canada, Inuit]{.mark} Tapiriit Kanatami, has
[collaborated with scientists to track]{.mark} the [effects]{.mark} of
climate change in the Arctic]{.underline} ([Sawatzky et al.,
2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib121)).
[[Satellite data and]{.mark} the Inuit people\'s [traditional
understanding of animal behaviour and sea ice conditions]{.mark} have
been used to [produce more precise climate models]{.mark}]{.underline}
([Huntington et al.,
2004](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib57)).
In a similar vein, Indigenous groups [[in Brazil]{.mark} have worked
with experts studying conservation to oversee biodiversity in [the
Amazon rainforest]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Souza and Garcia,
2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib131), [Fernández-Llamazares
et al.,
2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib42)).
[[Successful conservation outcomes have]{.mark} [resulted from]{.mark}
these [partnerships;]{.mark} native lands typically have lower rates of
deforestation]{.underline} than protected areas that are not native
([Nepstad et al.,
2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib93)).

[[Crop rotation, intercropping, and agroforestry are]{.mark} examples of
[indigenous]{.mark} agricultural [techniques]{.mark} that have been
established over generations [to provide food security and sustainable
land usage]{.mark}]{.underline}. For instance, to irrigate crops in arid
regions, the Zuni people of the American Southwest have created complex
water management systems ([Turner and Clifton,
2009](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib143)).
These methods have shown to be [[resilient in the face of climatic
unpredictability]{.mark}, providing insightful guidance for contemporary
agricultural systems.]{.underline}

Agrochemical treatments, precision farming, genetically modified
organisms (GMOs), and [other scientific advances in agriculture
have]{.underline} dramatically raised agricultural production. [However,
the environmental effects of these technologies---such as soil erosion,
biodiversity loss, and heightened reliance on chemical inputs---have
frequently drawn criticism]{.underline} ([Holt-Giménez and Altieri,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib55)).

[The [Shona people of Zimbabwe and]{.mark} participatory plant [breeding
initiatives in India]{.mark} are [combin]{.mark}ing [traditional methods
of seed preservation with]{.mark} scientific [soil fertility
analyses]{.mark} to [increase]{.mark} crop [yields and maintain soil
health]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Cleveland and Soleri,
2002](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib30)).
These methods aim to maximize fertilizer management and boost yield
while maintaining soil health. Both initiatives aim to reintroduce
ancient rice varieties and promote environmentally friendly farming
techniques ([Nyathi and Dhlamini,
2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib96)).

Indigenous health systems use holistic treatment methods considering
environmental, physical, and spiritual factors ([WHO,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib151)).
[Traditional healers use medicinal herbs and spiritual practices to
treat a wide range of illnesses]{.underline} ([WHO,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib151)).
Traditional medicine remains the primary form of treatment for
indigenous peoples, particularly in rural areas ([WHO,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib151)).
Modern scientific healthcare includes evidence-based methods, new
pharmaceuticals, and technological advancements, but often prioritizes
symptoms over overall health ([WHO,
2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib151)).

[The [integration]{.mark} of scientific and indigenous knowledge [in
healthcare]{.mark} has [led to more]{.mark} [efficient]{.mark} and
culturally relevant [therapies]{.mark}]{.underline} ([Shisana et al.,
2016](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib123)).
In South Africa, [patients can now access both Western and traditional
medical systems]{.underline} through traditional healers\' regulation
([Shisana et al.,
2016](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib123)).
In Canada, [traditional midwifery techniques have [improved mother and
baby health outcomes]{.mark}]{.underline} in isolated indigenous
communities ([Van Wagner et al.,
2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib147)).

consensus of the literature base**
