2.1. Literature search

[The [discussion adopts a]{.mark} desk-based [systematic review
approach]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Aromataris and Pearson
(2014)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib9) define
a systematic review as an evaluation of the evidence on a clearly stated
issue that [uses an organised and explicit procedure [to identify,
select, evaluate, and synthesise primary research]{.mark}]{.underline},
as well as [[extract and analyse data from]{.mark} the [studies]{.mark}
examined.]{.underline} This is seen as a technique of laying out and
[summarising tangible evidence]{.underline} to highlight the research's
aims and shortcomings, as well as [[to inform policy assessment]{.mark}
and future research]{.underline} ([Khan et al.,
2001](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib62), [Patrick
and Khalema,
2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib105)).

[[A comprehensive literature search was conducted across multiple
databases]{.mark} using specific topics and specified
keywords]{.underline} \"Indigenous knowledge,\" OR \"Scientific
knowledge,\" OR \"Integration,\" "Cultural sensitivity," OR "Oral
histories," OR "Traditional knowledge," OR "Knowledge transmission," OR
"Community-based knowledge," OR "risk mitigation," and OR \"traditional
practices\" using Scopus, Google Scholar, Web of Science, Emerald,
Connected papers, and Elsevier library. The data sourced were then
managed using Mendeley. The justification for using Google Scholar is
that it [[leads to resources]{.mark} that were [not initially]{.mark}
considered, expanding the search to resources that were not
[indexed]{.mark} in other databases.]{.underline} It is important to
note that Mendeley was not used as a primary search engine**.** Rather,
Mendeley functioned as a reference as well as a search management tool
to store, organize, and annotate citations retrieved from the primary
databases. It also facilitated the de-duplication of search results and
collaborative review among co-authors. Mendeley also provides resources
that were not initially considered via the search option. However, it
does not always provide an accurate database result which is a
limitation in terms of the study duplication. While the structured
database search is replicable, we acknowledge that some relevant studies
were identified through manual methods (i.e. Mendeley search)**,** such
as backward citation tracking and recommendations from grey literature.
These supplementary strategies, while valuable for ensuring thematic
exposure, may reduce replicability due to their subjective nature.

[The literature synthesis focused on the integration of IK with SK, the
constraints faced with the integration of IK and SK, and the
success(es)]{.underline} of practised knowledge system integration were
also examined. The keywords searched in the databases for the initial
phases of title and abstract screening produced a wide variety of
results. Not only were these terms searched in the article\'s title, but
also the general topic, abstracts, and keywords.

The topic search words used are shown in [Table
1](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#tbl0005).
Early searches encompassed a wide range of topics and word combinations.
The database searches were restricted to English-language literature
with no constraints on author or publication year. [[Peer-reviewed
publications]{.mark} such as research reports, policy notes (focal group
discussions included, community briefing included), [discussion/working
papers,]{.mark} [thesis and dissertations, books, conference papers, and
case studies were included]{.mark} in the initial article screening. The
inclusion criteria for this review required that studies focus on the
integration of Indigenous and scientific knowledge, include practical
applications, or [critically analyze the benefits and challenges of such
integration]{.mark}]{.underline}. Studies that did not meet these
criteria were excluded. A total of 951 articles were initially
identified through the keyword search. After title screening, 317
articles remained. Following abstract screening, this number was reduced
to 210. After a full-text review for relevance and alignment with the
inclusion criteria, [156 articles were selected for inclusion in the
final systematic review.]{.underline}

# Aff Answers 

## Solvency

### Consultation Fails\-\--Solvency

#### U.S. has empirically made and broken "legally binding" treaties to assert control.

NGC '25 (Native Governance Center, native-led nonprofit dedicated to
assisting Native governance, recognized with the Minnesota Council of
Nonprofits Mission Award for Anti-Racism Initiative "Understanding &
Using Treaty Rights Guide" May 14 2025,
https://nativegov.org/resources/understanding-using-treaty-rights-guide/
//vo)

Treaties are nation-to-nation agreements between two sovereign powers
that must be upheld. In this context, we focus on treaty agreements of
Native nations and the United States of America. Treaties are legally
binding contracts between sovereign nations that establish those
nations' political and property relations. Treaties between Native
nations and the United States confirm each nation's rights and
privileges. Like the Constitution and Bill of Rights, treaties do not
expire with time. They have the same power now as when they were
originally created. Article Six of the United States Constitution holds
that treaties "are the supreme law of the land" inferring that when a
treaty is signed, it is a legal document that cannot be broken.
**[[The]{.mark} [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [used treaties]{.mark}
mainly [as a means to take land]{.mark} and legitimize the transactions
[from]{.mark} and with [Native nations]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Others
were written to legitimize trade, military partnerships, and negotiate
peace with Native nations. Treaties were not entirely foreign to Native
nations prior to colonization as they had entered into treaties with
other Native nations regularly. [When treaties were made with the United
States, [Native nations often asked for]{.mark} specific
[provisions]{.mark} to be written into them. These provisions stipulated
certain material items in exchange for land]{.underline}. Many Native
nations also sought provisions that would retain a portion of land for
permanent homes for their citizens. Lands would then be reserved for
them and for their use leading to the term reservations which is used
today. [Today, **[we see]{.mark} that many of [these]{.mark} [treaties
have not been upheld]{.mark} or have been [broken on behalf of the
federal government]{.mark}**, and that has been done by design. Some of
**[these treaties were]{.mark} [signed for performative measures]{.mark}
[or intended to not]{.mark} be [follow]{.mark}ed [through]{.mark}**,
unbeknownst to the Native nations who agreed to them at the
time.]{.underline}

### Consultation Fails: No Spillover\-\--Solvency

#### No spill up, it won't be considered for implementation. 

**Romero Manrique  et al. 18** \[David Romero Manrique Lara,
Thomas Voelker, Jade Zoghbi, Ângela Martinho Guimarães Pereira;
August 13, 2018; acc. July 6, 2025; Researchers at the Joint Research
Centre of the European Commission (specializing in Arctic traditional
knowledge and policy engagement); Publications Office of the European
Union; "Arctic: Traditional Knowledge, Livelihoods and Community
Engagement -- Setting the
Scene"; https://www.europeanpolarboard.org/fileadmin/user_upload/Arctic\_\_Traditional_Knowledge\_\_EC\_\_2018.pdf;\]

Yet, our [review suggests]{.underline} that [[traditional
knowledge]{.underline}]{.mark} is [[insufficiently considered in Arctic
policy]{.mark} [making]{.mark}]{.underline}; much [more
[effort]{.mark}]{.underline} is [[needed]{.mark} in order [to

within the wider knowledge base that informs policy making at all
governance levels, since the [mere [representation]{.mark} of these
groups at international forums is [not enough to channel
t]{.mark}raditional [k]{.mark}nowledge [to]{.mark}
formulating]{.underline}, [implementing, and [monitor]{.mark}ing
[strategies to deal with c]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange impacts or
other environmental change not attributable to climate]{.underline}.

#### FPIC will never be adopted internationally \-\-- countries don't follow on

**Tagliarino et Al. 16** \[Nicholas Tagliarino is a researcher of land
and resource rights for LRR and procedural environmental rights for the
Environmental Democracy Index, Celine Salcedo-La Viña is a Senior
Associate at the Equity Center\'s Land and Resource Rights Initiative
and Gender Equity Practice, Sam Szoke-Burke is a senior legal researcher
for the Columbia Center on Sustainable Investment's, "Strengthening
Indigenous Land Rights: 3 Challenges to 'Free, Prior and Informed
Consent,'" 05/09/2016,
https://www.wri.org/insights/strengthening-indigenous-land-rights-3-challenges-free-prior-and-informed-consent#:\~:text=2.,safeguard%20their%20lands%20and%20resources.\]

Indigenous representatives from around the world are convening in New
York this week for the 15th session of the UN Permanent Forum on
Indigenous Issues. The Forum is the UN's central coordinating and
advisory body on indigenous issues, and the annual sessions provide the
opportunity to take stock of issues affecting Indigenous Peoples around
the world. One such issue is large-scale development projects' ongoing
threats to indigenous control of lands and resources.

Before the bulldozers arrive in their communities, [[indigenous]{.mark}
men and women [must be informed and given the opportunity to decide
whether or not to allow]{.mark} such [a project to happen]{.mark}. Under
international human rights law, this right to "free, prior and informed
consent" ([FPIC) applies to all development projects]{.mark} that
directly affect indigenous communities' access to their lands and
resources.]{.underline}

Looking back on the history and development of FPIC, one can see clear
signs of progress, but also significant challenges to ensuring this
right is realized. Here are three key obstacles to overcome:

1\. [[FPIC]{.mark} is an internationally recognized human right, but
[isn't]{.mark} always [treated]{.mark} that way [at the national
level.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[[While]{.mark} Indigenous Peoples' right to [FPIC is]{.mark} explicitly
[incorporated into]{.mark} both binding and non-binding [international
human rights laws, not all countries have adopted these
laws]{.mark}.]{.underline} For instance, the ILO Convention 169 on
Indigenous and Tribal Peoples is a legally binding treaty recognizing
indigenous rights to give consent, but it's only ratified by and thus
legally binding in 22 countries. Similarly, the legally binding
International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR) has been
ratified by 160 countries and has been authoritatively interpreted by
the UN Human Rights Committee to protect FPIC rights, but not all
countries embrace this interpretation. FPIC is also recognized in the UN
Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples and the Voluntary
Guidelines on Responsible Governance of Tenure of Land, Fisheries and
Forests (VGGTs), but [these "[soft laws" aren't technically legally
binding.]{.mark}]{.underline}

2\. [[Few countries have incorporated FPIC into their national
laws]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[[Thirty-six]{.mark} out of the 39 [countries]{.mark}]{.underline}
assessed on LandMark, a mapping platform of indigenous and community
lands and legal rights, [[do not have]{.mark} national [laws that
provide indigenous communities]{.mark} [with]{.mark} strong [legal
rights to FPIC]{.mark}. This is problematic given that [many of these
countries' laws grant]{.mark} governments broad legal [authority to take
indigenous land without]{.mark} consultation or
[consent]{.mark}]{.underline}. A forthcoming WRI working paper explores
how many countries' laws do not require governments to identify, inform
or consult affected landholders prior to acquiring land in a compulsory
manner, despite international standards calling for governments to do
so.

Of the countries assessed on LandMark, the Philippines, Colombia and
Peru are the only three that have incorporated strong FPIC rights for
indigenous communities in their national laws. Australia, Bolivia, South
Africa, South Sudan and Tanzania have also made some progress by
incorporating limited indigenous rights to give consent before their
lands are acquired by governments or other outsiders. If followed in
practice, these laws can protect indigenous communities from unwanted
encroachment and help them safeguard their lands and resources.

3\. FPIC is not always sensitive to gender issues.

[Even when recognized by domestic laws, [FPIC can have differing impacts
on men and women.]{.mark}]{.underline}

A recent study of FPIC implementation highlights how women are not
necessarily included in decision-making processes about land and
resources. Women in a community affected by a palm oil concession in
Desa Mekar Jaya, Indonesia, for example, stated that they had not heard
of FPIC, and it was mostly men who were involved in community
consultations related to the investment, despite the fact that women
work side-by-side with men in Indonesia's plantations[. The fact that
[women]{.mark} were [not consulted is]{.mark} particularly
[problematic]{.mark} given the risk of reproductive problems that can
arise from exposure to pesticides and other hazardous chemicals used by
the palm oil company]{.underline}. [Given [their role in]{.mark} rural
[communities]{.mark} and the impacts investments may have on them, [it
is important for women to be part of the FPIC
process.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[Inter]{.mark}national standards---such as the VGGTs and the
International Finance Corporation Performance Standards (IFC), which
must be complied with by all investors financed by the IFC--- now
specifically recognize that FPIC must capture the views of both men and
women, but it's clear that there's still more work to do to achieve
gender equity in this area.

5. Challenges in integrating indigenous and scientific knowledge

to]{.mark} their distinct [epistemological differences. SK is
factual]{.mark} and generic, [while IK is experiential and
holistic]{.mark}]{.underline} ([[Agrawal,
1995]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001352#bib2)).

scientific organizations can [hinder integration]{.mark}
efforts]{.underline}. Successful integration requires equitable
relationships that honor and cherish IK, as [[historically]{.mark}
suppressed [SK]{.mark} systems [have been
overlooked]{.mark}]{.underline} ([[Nakashima and Roué,

[p]{.mark}roperty [r]{.mark}ights. [Indigenous knowledge is owned
collectively, yet individual ownership and patenting are]{.mark}
typically [the focus of scientific study]{.mark}.]{.underline} Careful

property while enabling the incorporation of their knowledge into

#### Traditional Knowledge is overlooked by experts and won't be used.

**Gricius 24** \[Gabriella Gricius; April 29, 2024; July 6, 2025; PhD

issue area. [Byers (Reference Byers2017) specifically mentioned the role
of experts on technical issues including international law and geology
as essential for how states make extended continental shelf claims as
well as fisheries claims such as the Norwegian-Russian Fisheries
Commissi]{.underline}on. Mason & Soilkova (Reference Mason and

"[experts]{.mark}."]{.underline}

5.2.1.1. Procedural limitations

[[A key limitation to]{.underline}]{.mark} company- and [state-led
[consultation]{.mark} and consent [is procedural]{.mark}]{.underline},
with studies highlighting issues [around the goals, scope, and timing of
engagement. [Communities]{.mark}]{.underline} often [[perceive
consultations as]{.mark} bureaucratic [box-ticking
exercises]{.mark}]{.underline} \[[117]{.underline}\], [[with
pre-determined agendas]{.mark} aimed at project approval [rather than
genuine dialogue]{.mark}]{.underline}. Accommodations are typically
limited to project terms and conditions, [[framing the question as "how"
rather than "whether" to execute a project]{.mark}]{.underline}
\[[33]{.underline}\]. In terms of timing, [there is a perception that
[engagement: does not start early enough]{.mark} in the planning
process]{.underline} \[[99]{.underline},[118]{.underline}\]; [[leaves
insufficient time]{.mark} for preparation, discussion and addressing
concerns; [and is inadequate in engaging]{.mark} vulnerable and often
[excluded groups]{.mark}]{.underline}, such as youth
\[[119]{.underline}\] or women \[[89]{.underline}\]. In Greenland, for
example, "insufficient, late and overly narrow public participation are
major themes in the decision phase of the natural resource projects"
\[[120]{.underline}\]. A 2014 report by the Greenland NGO "Coalition for
Better Citizen Involvement" made recommendations for improving citizen
engagement \[[96]{.underline}\], [yet [procedural issues]{.mark}
continue to [undermine effective and meaningful communication, impacting
the ability]{.mark} of communities and stakeholders [to reach agreeable
decisions]{.mark} \[99\].]{.underline}

**[Legal ambiguity]{.underline}\-\--among Indigenous people over land
complicate consultation and consent**

5.2.1.2. Legal ambiguity and gaps

[[Another limitation]{.mark} refers to [legal ambiguity]{.mark} and gaps
[regarding land]{.mark} and property [rights]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Studies highlight [the issues of [overlapping and unresolved land claims
and legal interests across territories and]{.mark} among multiple
[owner]{.mark}s and land users]{.underline}
\[[89]{.underline},[121]{.underline}\]. [This [creates confusion
regarding the rights and responsibilities of
stakeholders]{.mark},]{.underline} contributing to disputes and
[[challenges]{.mark} in achieving [meaningful
consent]{.mark}]{.underline}. In addition, there are
[[uncertainties]{.mark} or inadequacies in legal frameworks concerning
triggers [for consultation]{.mark} and consent.]{.underline} In Russia,
for example, "the federal regulation requires companies to hold public
hearings with local communities to discuss environmental impacts as part
of EIA \[environmental impact assessment\] at the stage of state project
approval. However, the federal regulation does not require a specific
consultation with Indigenous peoples" and there are no requirements for
Indigenous consent \[[78]{.underline}\].

#### Barriers to FPIC \-\-- Identification, Corruption, and Banks deck solvency 

**Baker 13** \[Shalanda Baker is a first vice provost for sustainability
and climate action at the University of Michigan, "WHY THE IFC'S FREE,

https://wilj.law.wisc.edu/wp-content/uploads/sites/1270/2014/04/Baker_print.pdf\]

[A [broad reading of FPIC]{.mark} would [give]{.mark} indigenous
[communities]{.mark} full [veto power]{.mark} over projects.99
[This]{.mark} [view is]{.mark} currently [not supported]{.mark} by the
IFC]{.underline},100 but such an interpretation could provide real

order [to]{.mark} [obtain]{.mark} their [consent or]{.mark} the
[potential for widespread corruption and co-opting of community

indigenous peoples affected by development projects. Despite this

unrealistic]{.mark} given the timelines involved in large-scale project

until]{.mark} the [IFC or a bank bound by FPIC]{.mark} vis-à-vis the

would]{.mark} ever [endorse the veto interpretation]{.mark}, especially
without a mechanism in place to compensate the developer for its sunk

I now turn to the more likely interpretation of FPIC--- consultation
"plus."

#### Isolation, internal participation, and overlap make FPIC wholly ineffective

**McCulloch 21** \[Emily McCulloch; University of Montana -- Public Land
Resource Review, "Free, Prior, and Informed Consent: A Struggling

[[Fundamental issues remain with implementing]{.mark} the [FPIC]{.mark}

[[Isolation is detrimental]{.mark} to the FPIC's goals [because]{.mark}
some indigenous [populations desire to live

[[public participation and consultation are
difficult]{.underline}]{.mark}.64 Scholars argue the "desire to be left
alone" shows they do not give consent to projects because of their
intention to secure their land from the outside communities.65

This reluctance and mistrust lend itself to difficulties when
implementing the FPIC. [[Another]{.mark} issue with the FPIC

[[indigenous groups contain marginalized]{.mark} [populations]{.mark}

[[A third]{.mark} major [issue]{.mark} with implementation [is overlap

### Consultation Fails --- Land Rights

#### [Consultation fails]{.underline} --- it doesn't grant the [right to refuse]{.underline} external access to Indigenous land.

**Townsend \'20** \[Leo Townsend; British Academy Newton Fellow at the
University of Reading, PhD at the Centre for the Study of Mind in Nature
in Oslo; \"Consultation, Consent, and the Silencing of Indigenous
Communities\"; Wiley Online Library; May 25, 2020;
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/japp.12438\] // K Nahm

[The **right to [consultation]{.mark}**]{.underline}, discussed in the
previous two case studies, [[is a]{.mark} **procedural
[participatory]{.mark}** [**right**]{.mark}]{.underline}. It [[requires
decision-makers to **follow** certain procedures]{.underline}]{.mark}
([to consult with communities]{.underline}) [[and]{.mark} to [take
certain information into account]{.mark} in decision-making]{.underline}
(submissions and statements made by those communities in the course of
the consultation process). [It is [not a right that determines **the
outcomes** of the decision-making process]{.mark}]{.underline}; [[it
does not guarantee]{.mark} communities [continued occupation of]{.mark}
their [territories nor]{.mark}]{.underline} does it grant them **[[the
right to refuse]{.underline}]{.mark}** mining companies [[access to
their land]{.underline}]{.mark}. For this reason, many see [the right to
consultation]{.underline} as [**too weak** to meaningfully protect
Indigenous communities]{.underline}, and as a result [Indigenous rights
activists have argued that the state should not simply consult but also
obtain the free, prior and informed consent]{.underline} (FPIC) of the
community before making decisions that will impact its land and
livelihood. As a consequence of these efforts, the right to FPIC has now
been recognized in international legal texts and by the Inter-American
Court of Human Rights.

### Consultation Fails --- Agency Circumvention

#### [Agency discretion]{.underline} circumvents consultation.

**Sorgen et al. 24** \[Jeremy Sorgen, Peter Nelson, Van Butsic, Seth
LaRosa, Shasta Gaughen, Earl Crosby, Robert Geary, Jennifer Sowerwine;
\" Unchecking the box: Overcoming barriers to meaningful consultation\";
Wiley Online Library; Aug 5, 2024;
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001017?dgcid=rss_sd_all\]
// K Nahm

[Agency discretion refers to the power of a government agency to decide
**whether and** how to implement laws and regulations]{.underline}.
According to California law, Tribes possess the right to consult when
proposed development projects threaten Tribal cultural resources. But
[in practice, [agencies exercise **wide latitude** to decide what
consultation entails]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [what merits
consideration]{.underline}]{.mark} as a Tribal cultural resource, [[what
counts as sufficient]{.mark} mitigation]{.underline} of anticipated
impacts, [[and]{.underline}]{.mark} even [[what projects are
exempt]{.underline}]{.mark} from the requirement to consult. If a
resource is not already listed on the California Register of Historical
Resources (and many are not for reasons that we explain in the section
below on knowledge gaps), the onus is on Tribes to convince agency
counterparts of the merits of their claim: "You have to get a lead
agency for a project doing CEQA to concur the site was actually a site"
(TR 1).4 Sharing a similar sentiment, an agency representative observed:
"When I read AB 52, the fact is that the lead agency can listen to a
Tribe through a consultation process and say, 'We disagree. We're going
forward.' That is bizarre" (AR 2). When an agency, in their discretion,
determines that the landscape feature in question does not merit
consideration as a Tribal cultural resource or that impacts do not
exceed certain "thresholds of significance" or that removal or
compensation is a sufficient mitigation measure, [[Tribes that disagree
have **little recourse** but to sue]{.mark} in court, [which is]{.mark}
very [costly]{.mark}, [time-consuming]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} historically
has [not led to favorable outcomes]{.mark} for Tribes]{.underline}.
Further upstream, [[agencies can]{.mark} also [exercise]{.mark} their
[discretion by creating]{.mark} a permit [process that circumvents
consultation or delays it to]{.mark} the point that consultation would
[be **meaningless**]{.mark}]{.underline}. In the case of cannabis, some
counties created a regulatory category of "interim" or "provisional
permit" or "early activation," which allowed cannabis operators to
continue cultivation without completing the permit process.5 Under these
temporary permits, operators sometimes cultivated cannabis for years
without undergoing CEQA review (AR 2). Since AB 52 is part of CEQA,
these projects would not be subject to Tribal consultation: "We were
getting notifications for projects and going out there and things had
already started. They had already dug their utility lines or their
security shack or water lines...And so it was just a free for all,
everybody was getting these early activations" (TR 2). Similarly, some
counties and most cities used "ministerial approvals" for cannabis, that
is, expedited review at the administrative level based on whether a
project conforms to applicable building and zoning code requirements.
For example, a small project on private property that does not meet a
threshold level of ground disturbance may not require additional review.
Like provisional licenses, ministerial permits bypass CEQA and deny
Tribes the opportunity to consult under AB 52. The State intervened in
some cases that they found too hasty (such as in Inyo County), yet many
projects were allowed or had already begun on which Tribes would have
liked to consult. In short, making consultation a part of other statutes
rather than a standalone law, for example subsuming AB 52 under CEQA,
creates legislative loopholes and ultimately limits the applicability of
cultural resource laws, further strengthening agencies' discretionary
authority to determine whether and how these laws apply.6

#### Agencies have [no incentive]{.underline} for complying --- weak enforcement of agreements means consultation goes [nowhere]{.underline}. 

**Sorgen et al. 24** \[Jeremy Sorgen, Peter Nelson, Van Butsic, Seth
LaRosa, Shasta Gaughen, Earl Crosby, Robert Geary, Jennifer Sowerwine;
\" Unchecking the box: Overcoming barriers to meaningful consultation\";
Wiley Online Library; Aug 5, 2024;
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1462901125001017?dgcid=rss_sd_all\]
// K Nahm

[[Weak enforcement]{.mark} mechanisms]{.underline} tend to
[[have]{.underline}]{.mark} the same effect as [legislative [loopholes
and limitations]{.mark}]{.underline} placed on the scope of policies,
all of [[which remove incentives for upholding]{.mark} cultural
[resource laws]{.mark}]{.underline}. As one Tribal representative
explained, "It's [[almost meaningless to have]{.mark} permit [conditions
**if no one is checking to see if they are
followed**]{.mark}]{.underline}" (TR 3). A common refrain among cultural
resource practitioners is that these [laws "lack teeth,"]{.underline}
that is, [there are [few consequences for agencies that fail to
comply]{.mark}]{.underline}. For example, one of the main regulatory
authorities, the State Water Resources Control Board, noted in an
interview that it uses a complaint-based system, which is relatively
strong on inspections prior to issuing permits and light on compliance
checks after the fact (AR 3). A lack of enforcement renders cultural
resource laws non-binding, which gives agencies wide latitude to apply
the law as they see fit. A Tribal Chair said: "I think AB 52 has some
good bones to it. The premise and the structure of it are good. It's
just follow-through from agencies. I don't know how you change the
mentality of, well, this is where...it says I need to check that box (TR
4).

[[Weak enforcement has downstream effects on]{.mark} other aspects of
project approvals and [development]{.mark}]{.underline}. Several
interviewees described scenarios in which
[[developers]{.underline}]{.mark} were able to [[remain vague
about]{.mark} project start [dates or did not keep]{.mark} their
[agreement]{.mark} to have cultural monitors onsite at the start of

Last year, a judge ruled against Arizona-based tribes, Tohono O'odham
Nation and San Carlos Apache Tribe, in a suit that was challenging the
construction of the SunZia transmission line in southern Arizona's San
Pedro Valley. The line passes through an area that holds historic,

been fighting its construction for years, advocating that it be built
alongside land that has already been developed. It crosses "one of the
most intact cultural landscapes in the Southwest," a 2024 lawsuit by the
Tohono O'odham Nation stated. The project was a cornerstone of former
President Joe Biden's green energy agenda.

investments from President Biden's Investing in America agenda, the
Interior Department is helping build modern, resilient climate
infrastructure that protects our communities from the worsening impacts
of climate change."

The case was appealed to the Court of the Appeals for the Ninth Circuit.
The panel of judges heard oral arguments on March 26. An opinion has yet
to be released.

## Net-Benefit

### AT: Net-Benefit\-\--Squo Solves\-\--Minerals Consultation 

#### SQUO solves the net-benefit\-\--FPIC [already exists]{.underline} in mining projects.

**Anna K. Bidgood and Jessica Hall 24 \[11/18/2024; Anna K. Bidgood**
(Carnegie Institution for Science, Metamorphic, economic, structural
geologist/geochemist); Jessica Hall(Centre for Sámi Studies, UiT The
Arctic University of Norway); Earth Science, Systems and Society; "We
Need to Talk About Mining in the Arctic,";
https://www.lyellcollection.org/doi/full/10.3389/esss.2024.10117\]//lol

Social Movements and Legislation

[[Throughout the Arctic,]{.mark} Indigenous and local [communities have
had their right to self-determination continually thwarted and
challenged]{.mark} by a myriad of industries and developmental projects
in the region.]{.underline} [The sentiment behind ["Green
Colonialism,"]{.mark} the [purposeful sacrificing of Indigenous land
in]{.mark} the [name of environmental goals]{.mark}, has [led to the
creation of]{.mark} domestic and international [agreements with the aim
of protecting and preserving Indigenous land, culture and
identity.]{.mark} Development of land claim settlements, such as the
Alaska Native Land Claim Settlement in 1971, the largest Indigenous land
settlement in US history, have positively contributed to Impact and
Benefit Agreements between Indigenous peoples and mines in Alaska and
elsewhere.]{.underline}

[Whilst [the mining sector]{.mark} is an industry [known to have
a]{.mark} direct [impact on Indigenous communities in the
Arctic,]{.mark} the impacts of development in the Arctic come from a
wider array of industries [including but not limited to]{.mark} resource
[extraction, exploitation]{.mark} of other natural resources (hunting,
fishing), [tourism, transport and]{.mark} energy
[infrastructure]{.mark}.]{.underline} These impacts have led to the
development of a variety of frameworks and legislation which have been
previously affected by lack of recognition of the rights of Indigenous
peoples to land, mineral and natural resources.

One example of this is The United Nations Declaration on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP) which was adopted in 2007 and establishes a
universal framework of minimum standards for the survival, dignity and
wellbeing of Indigenous Peoples worldwide (United Nations, 2015). UNDRIP
stipulates that Indigenous Peoples are not solely stakeholders in
potential extractive projects but instead, they are right holders. The
ratification of UNDRIP led to change throughout the Arctic. For example,
in Greenland, the 30 year old "Greenland Home Rule Arrangement" was
replaced in 2009 by the Act on Greenland Self Government. This provided
the legal basis for Greenland gaining authority, previously held by
Denmark, in matters exclusively related to Greenlandic domestic affairs
and natural resources (International Energy Agency, 2023). Furthermore,
in 2011, the Government of Sweden added a reform to the Constitution,
stipulating that the Government shall encourage opportunities for the
Sámi people and for other ethnic, linguistic and religious minorities in
Sweden to preserve and develop their own culture and community life
(Human Rights Council Report, 2014).

[An integral part of UNDRIP is the notion of Free, Prior and Informed
Consent ([FPIC]{.mark}) which [stipulates]{.mark} that [states must have
obtained free, prior and informed consent before adopting and
implementing]{.mark} legislative or administrative [measures which
impact Indigenous Peoples]{.mark}. The [recognition and
promotion]{.mark} of the Free Prior Informed Consent is an important
principle of engaging with Indigenous peoples [during mining
projects]{.mark} and [has become]{.mark} very [important]{.mark}
worldwide.]{.underline} The International Finance Corporation, a member
of the World Bank, has created eight performance standards which aim to
provide guidance on how to avoid, mitigate and manage risks so that
business is conducted in a sustainable way (International Finance
Corporation, 2012a). [These standards recognise that Indigenous Peoples
may be particularly vulnerable to the adverse impacts associated with
project development, including the loss of identity, culture, and
natural resource-based livelihoods (International Finance Corporation,
2012b). The [implementation of FPIC has also been seen in the mining
industry.]{.mark}]{.underline} In 2013, the International Council on
Mining and Metals (ICMM) published a position statement on the rights of
Indigenous peoples incorporating FPIC principles and promoting
constructive relationships between mining companies and Indigenous
peoples. ICMM noted the increase in acknowledgement and application of
FPIC in the mining industry since the Mining, Minerals and Sustainable
Development (MMSD) initiative was completed, 12 years ago.

[In addition to the protections provided by UNDRIP and FPIC, the
International Labour Organisation Convention formed the Indigenous and
Tribals Peoples Convention in 1989, which is often referred to as ILO
No. 169, recognising Indigenous Peoples' right to self-determination and
paving the way for the creation and implementation of
UNDRIP.]{.underline} A previous ILO convention, No. 107 formed in 1957,
was created in the wake of WW2 amidst rising concern regarding the
oppression and discrimination of Indigenous Peoples. Norway declined to
sign this convention in 1958, noting that the provisions included did
not apply to the Sámi (Ravna, 2020) but became the first to ratify ILO
No. 169 in 1989. As one of the signatory states, Norway is obliged to
recognise Sámi self-determination and within this is the restitution of
Sámi culture and heritage. An example of this is the Bååstede project
which has seen around 1,600 Sámi cultural heritage artefacts from the
Norwegian Folk Museum and the Museum of Cultural History in Oslo, being
returned to six Sámi museum siidas. Furthermore, the National Archives
of Norway handed over the Lapp Codicil which is an addendum to the
Stromstad Treaty of 1751 which defined the Norwegian-Swedish border, to
the Sámi Archives, located in Kautokeino in 2021. This addendum to the
border treaty contains provisions on the right of reindeer-herding
between the Swedish and Norwegian border (The Library of Congress,
2021).

**S/quo solves -- consultative measures with Natives are already being
put forward in mining**

**US Department of Indian Affairs 23** \[Bureau of Indian Affairs, a
United States federal agency within the Department of the Interior;
"National Tribal Critical Minerals Initiative,"
https://www.bia.gov/service/tribal-consultations/national-tribal-critical-minerals-initiative#:\~:text=The%20purpose%20of%20this%20initiative,development%20or%20other%20benefits%20sharing.\]//AChiong

In February 2021, President Biden announced an Executive Order on
securing America's supply chains - including critical minerals - to
further our nation's national security and climate goals including a
transition net zero emissions economy by 2050. Critical minerals are the
building blocks of this energy transition and the Biden-Harris
Administration (the "Administration") is pursuing a whole-of-government
approach to increase the reliability of the critical minerals supply
chain, including strengthening recycling, researching critical minerals
replacements, assessing standards, pursuing international engagements,
and increasing responsible domestic mining.

[[The Administration acknowledges]{.mark} the [problematic and damaging
history between extractive industries and Tribal communities]{.mark},
and the potential impact of new mining to significantly impact Tribal
lands, people and resources.]{.underline} Early, meaningful, and ongoing
consultation and engagement with Tribal governments is imperative as the

supply chain. Furthermore, [[Tribes should]{.mark} be given the
opportunity to [equitably share]{.mark} in the economic development
[benefits]{.mark} of mineral development.]{.underline}

First and foremost, the Administration is striving to not repeat the
sins of the past with extractive industry. As part of this effort, the
Administration's Department of the Interior-led Interagency Working
Group (IWG) on Mining Laws, Regulations, and Permitting held four Tribal
consultations and other Tribal listening sessions to receive Tribal
input on improving mining on public lands. In September 2023, the IWG
published the Recommendations to Improve Mining on Public Lands report
with dozens of recommendations for improving Tribal consultation and
engagement, protection of Tribal resources, and sharing of economic
benefits when it comes to mining. Note, the report's recommendations may
also support improved practices for projects not on public lands.

[In response to several Tribal inquiries about the Administration's

### AT: Net-Benefit\-\--Self-D\-\--Impact Turn

[As such, it appears pressing that FPIC is defined and advocated for in

This brief discussion reveals how [quickly one can become engaged in
dissecting, rearticulating and advocating for FPIC so that Indigenous

Kathy Bowrey is right to acknowledge that '[what legal institutions are
and how they function remain very much under-theorized in much of the
literature about Indigenous peoples and the law'.]{.underline}17 Despite
an abundance of literature on Indi-genous peoples and international law,
and partly as an effect of that literature's disciplinary focus and its
disciplinary effects, [the untroubled nature of the sub-jectivity that
claims FPIC remains undertheorized.]{.underline} 18 That, in itself, is
troubling, which means that it is dangerous, not that it is necessarily
bad.19

This book takes Bowrey's concern seriously. It argues that [scholars,
advocates and others who participate in international legal discourse
have not analyzed how they use the discourse of international human
rights law to produce the subjects who are supposed to claim
FPIC.]{.underline} 20 To explain how scholars and others have
underestimated the effects of international legal discourse, I use a
legal performative methodology to problematize or trouble the
subject-status of those who claim Indigenous peoples' rights and the
role of scholars, institutions, NGOs and others in producing that
subject-status. As more fully explained in Chapter 1, I borrow primarily
from Judith Butler and Michel Foucault, as well as a few others, to the-
orize how the international legal discourses produced and produces
rights and the subjects who are supposed to claim them. [Although many
[self-identifying Indi-genous peoples are]{.mark} well [aware of human
rights limitations and power disparities]{.mark},]{.underline} 21 [this
methodology enriches the analysis of power by squarely addressing the
[lim-itations of international human rights law]{.mark} as well as the
ways in which scholars and others who use discourses of power and law
produce intuitively appealing, alluring [and]{.mark} yet [problematic
approaches to rights claiming.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Although Butler addresses Indigenous peoples only in a limited context,
22 her performative approach to gender assists in understanding the
demands of the law placed on subjects. 23 As articulated here, [a
performative method pro- vides a means of theorizing, and hence viewing
the performative enactment, or the subjectification, to international
legal discourse as identifiable Indigenous peoples that occurs through
invoking FPIC.]{.underline} We will see that [in order to be
identifiable as a representative of Indigenous peoples for the purposes
of claiming FPIC, [individuals and communities insert
themselves]{.mark}, as others insert them, [into international legal
discourse, one]{.mark} that is [produced and reproduced by scholars,
advocates, self-identifying Indigenous peoples and
others.]{.mark}]{.underline} This often occurs when rights claimants
assert that they are traditional and essential Indigenous peoples. To do
so does not mean separating oneself from the coercion of states,
industry, NGOs or others. Rather, it requires constructing oneself as a
subject of international legal discourse in dependence upon others,
those coercive forces and disciplinary powers so that others can
identify one as a bearer of human rights. [This methodology evaluates
how [individuals and com- munities become subjects]{.mark} who bear
those rights [to reveal oppressive, agentic and potentially subversive
uses of those rights.]{.mark}]{.underline}

To be seen as bearing Indigenous peoples' human rights is to
performatively act in a way that is identifiable to international legal
discourse participants. That does not mean it is false or fake, nor that
it is only negative or oppressive. It does mean that becoming Indigenous
peoples is not merely or solely an individual choice or decision.
Performatively enacting a legal subjectivity is contingent upon others
legitimating and confirming the applicability of international legal
discourse to that situation. Accordingly, one must also consider how
scholars, advocates and others -- who might be called 'elites' or
'experts'24 -- use rights, as well as consider how those who claim human
rights performatively enact sub-jectivities of Indigenous peoples within
international legal discourse. [Examining high profile [situations
involving FPIC and self-determination]{.mark} claims [shows]{.mark} that
rights claiming can be [both oppressive and agentic at the same
time]{.mark}, and, fur-ther, that the possibility of insurrectionary
potential resides in Indigenous peo-ples claiming FPIC and
self-determination. However, [it is troubling that anyone presupposes
that there are subjects called 'Indigenous peoples' who possess]{.mark}
objects called ['human rights']{.mark} that pre-exist international
legal discourse on Indigenous peoples and human rights. In short, the
legal performative method provides a conceptual framework for rethinking
the promise and power of Indi-genous peoples' rights, and by extension
human rights law. This has con- sequences in terms of language and
terminology, but it also has effects that are not obvious to me or
anyone else.]{.underline}

The first consequence is a terminological issue, which begins to
highlight the extent and depth of these troubles. [[We colloquially
deploy the term 'Indigen-ous peoples' as a blanket term to refer to all
tribal, native, Aboriginal, First Nations, nomadic, American Indian,
historically land-connected subsistence farmers, or other
peoples.]{.mark}]{.underline} 25 [But, [for the purposes of claiming
human rights, Indigenous peoples are and must be subjects of
international legal discourse.]{.mark} The term ['Indigenous
peoples']{.mark} can be -- and often is -- deployed in a lay sense, as a
naturalized signifier, which [is a term of a 'universalizing and
neutralizing rhetoric' of law]{.mark}.]{.underline} 26 [However, this
legal performative methodology evaluates how international legal
discourse 'hides' that Indigenous peoples are its sub- jects, because
legal discourse conceals its own effects so that its subjects appear
natural.]{.underline}

Juridical power inevitably 'produces' what it claims merely to
represent; hence, politics must be concerned with this dual function of
power: the jur-idical and the productive. In effect, the law produces
and then conceals the notion of 'a subject before the law' in order to
invoke that discursive forma-tion as a naturalized foundational premise
that subsequently legitimates that law's own regulatory hegemony.27

[The legal performative method applied here is concerned with dual
functions of power: how legal discourse produces and then conceals the
notion of the subject. And [for the purposes of claiming human
rights]{.mark}, the signifier ['Indigenous peoples' is an international
legal subject that has not broken]{.mark} -- and necessarily cannot
break -- [with international legal discourse]{.mark}.]{.underline} [That
does not foreclose the possibility that the term 'Indigenous peoples'
has social utility or disciplinary effect in other discourses. It does
mean [if the subjectivity were to fully break]{.mark} from international
legal discourse[, then whatever human rights were claimed would not be
identifi- able, intelligible or legible to others as human rights
claims.]{.mark} There is no term other than 'Indigenous peoples' to
signify all those peoples without somehow also referencing their
international legal subjectivity and therein reinforcing interna- tional
law.]{.underline}28

And this is the problem: how then can anyone now refer to all those
peoples before they were Indigenous peoples and without presupposing
that, today, they all want to be subjects of international law? How can
we refer to those 'abject beings, those who are not yet "subjects" but
form the constitutive outside to the domain of the subject'? 29
[Commonly deployed terms are problematic for understanding how rights
claims, such as FPIC, are made, because, [in order to claim FPIC as a
right, claimants must be identifiable and intelligible as Indigen- ous
peoples.]{.mark} And to be identifiable as a ['claimant' presupposes the
ability to make a claim, which signifies]{.mark} the claimant as already
[a representative of Indi-genous peoples]{.mark}. This circularity
demonstrates how power precedes and produces subjects which then
conceals that production by limiting what can be discussed intelligibly.
30]{.underline} [An effect is that the ability to claim FPIC is
dependent on others recognizing the claimant as representing Indigenous
peoples -- the struggle to be identifiable as an international legal
subject becomes, in fact, part of the struggle in claiming FPIC. 31
Because it is impossible to discuss those who seek to claim FPIC without
presupposing that they are already Indigenous peoples, this work refers
to those who may not yet be identified as Indigenous peoples as 'auto-
chthonous communities', but only where there is not a more appropriate
loca-lized term.]{.underline}

[Admittedly, the term 'autochthonous communities' is inadequate because
there is no adequate term.]{.underline}32 It is inadequate because this
book excavates international models', which is further explained in
Chapter 1. That chapter explains that [a disciplinary effect of legal
discourse is that scholars tend to adopt partial pictures of power
called legal models, which allow them to treat FPIC or Indigenous rights
as standalone objects of legal inquiry that inure to pre-existing
natural subjects. Adopting legal models conceals that the subjects who
are supposed to claim those rights are also produced, constituted and
influenced by that discourse.]{.underline}

A third consequence of a performative methodology is that terms, even
legal terms and rights claims, cannot be fixed -- as in 'mended' or
'corrected', as well as 'stabilized' or 'settled' -- especially if those
terms are useful for creating con- troversies.36 This matters because,
to various degrees, scholars who deploy legal models are, in numerous
ways, attempting to 'fix' the status and definition of FPIC so that
Indigenous peoples can use FPIC to determine and have control over their
own forms of living. [But FPIC's problems cannot be fixed by linguistic
legal parsing. Furthermore, [some attempts to fix FPIC engage in]{.mark}
historically ana- chronistic [uses of terms such as 'Indigenous
peoples', 'self-determination' and 'FPIC'.]{.mark}]{.underline} 37 The
focus on fixing FPIC elides how scholars insert themselves within
discourse to engage in productive and formative aspects of a
discipline.38 [They do not merely use language -- [they
self-discipline]{.mark}, as they are socially disciplined, to be heard
and identified as speakers of international legal discourse.
Undoubtedly, many if not all scholars are aware that they are engaging
in a debate (if not dis- course) and are attempting to create better
uses of law for Indigenous peoples. The problem is that [scholars who
attempt to fix FPIC]{.mark} perform as though they are sovereign-masters
of language who [can dominate controversy, disambiguate ambiguity, and
master all potential significations so that a term can be stabilized to
work for Indigenous peoples as Indigenous peoples desire.]{.mark} To the
extent that any scholar is successful in fixing FPIC -- which I maintain
is performatively impos- sible -- they would negate the possibility that
FPIC claimants can assert or engen- der their own versions of
self-determination. If a scholar wishes to uphold Indigenous peoples as

mining sovereign peoples, or, otherwise, if a scholar were successful in

[[If one were to succeed in fixing FPIC]{.mark} so that it could be used

**SPF 24** \[12/11/24, Sasakawa Peace Foundation, The Sasakawa Peace

and intractable they become as layers of grievance and claims build up

### AT: Net-Benefit\-\--Self-D\-\--Defense 

#### Consultation isn't enough for self-determination -- giving back the land is key

**Klein et al. 23** \[10/23, Lawrence Robert Klein was an American
economist, María Jesús Muñoz-Torres is a Professor of Finance and
Accounting, María Ángeles Fernández-Izquierdo is a Professor of Finance
and Accounting, "A comparative account of indigenous participation in
extractive projects: The challenge of achieving Free, Prior, and
Informed Consent, " Elsevier,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214790X2300059X\]MZ

Unfortunately, there is not one clear definition of the meaning of
'consent' and this is what makes most companies introduce mere window
dressing policies to their codes of conduct and keeps them from fully
integrating the principle of Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC)
into their corporate culture. FPIC has been identified as the 'gold
standard' of community engagement because it requires 'the highest form
of participation of local stakeholders in development projects' (FAO,
2016: 50). FPIC provides a means to [[indigenous peoples to]{.mark} put
[their right to self-determination]{.mark} into practice and '[goes
beyond consultation']{.mark}, [a participatory right]{.mark} that is
traditionally regarded 'an individual right of each
citizen']{.underline} (Cambou, 2019: 40). Consultation, as it applies to
indigenous peoples, forms part of their collective rights as distinct
polities and requires both states and companies to rethink current
governance mechanisms around community participation.
[[Self-determination implies]{.mark} that [indigenous peoples]{.mark}
can [autonomously]{.mark} [govern their 'internal and local
affairs']{.mark},2 define 'their economic, social and cultural
development']{.underline} (Cambou, 2019: 34) [and [regain control over
the lands]{.mark}, territories, and [natural resources they have]{.mark}
traditionally occupied and managed]{.underline} (Szablowski, 2010;
Barelli, 2012; Cambou, 2019). 'FPIC is not only a question of process,
but also of outcome' (Anaya et al., 2017: 5) and must include the option
of withholding consent and withdrawing consent at any stage of the
project cycle (FAO, 2016; UNPFII, 2005).

While companies 'are beginning to recognize that consultations are not
optional' (Anaya and Puig, 2017: 447), it is precisely because of its
connection to the right to self-determination and the right to give or
withhold consent, that [FPIC is often interpreted as a sort of 'veto
power' held by indigenous peoples]{.underline}, prompting [[concerns
connected to]{.underline}]{.mark} national sovereignty, [[state control
over natural resources]{.mark}, and corporate economic
objectives]{.underline} (Cambou, 2019; Leydet, 2019; Tomlinson, 2019;
Szablowski, 2010). Concerning Article 32.2. on FPIC and the exploitation
of natural resources in the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP, 2007), indigenous peoples apply a
'purposive' interpretation, which sees 'consent as a mandatory
requirement' of the FPIC process, while companies and states generally
take a 'textual approach' to the article, which identifies FPIC 'as an
extension of consultation processes' (Papillon et al., 2020: 225). [In
operational terms this means that companies tend to apply an
'instrumentalist approach' to consultation]{.underline}, which they
identify as a 'participatory mechanism' analogical to a
'notice-and-comment' procedure, thus overshadowing 'concern for any
substantive rights (Anaya and Puig, 2017: 448). At worse, companies take
a 'minimalist approach' to consultation, converting it into a
'check-the-box' activity with consultations constituting 'a sizeable
bureaucratic obstacle that hinders productive activity' (Anaya and Puig,
2017: 450[). Both [approaches clash with indigenous peoples' view
of]{.mark} prior [consultation]{.mark}, as entailing the possibility to
say 'yes' or 'no' to a project affecting them.]{.underline}

### AT: Net Benefit\-\--CIL 

#### [CIL FAILS]{.underline}---CIL defines [broad norms]{.underline}, never [individual]{.underline} law. Inherent [imprecision]{.underline} destroys enforcement.

**Hakimi '20** --- Monica; James V. Campbell Professor of Law and
Associate Dean for Faculty and Research at the University of Michigan
Law School, JD from Yale Law School, BA from Duke University. 2020;
"Making Sense of Customary International Law"; Michigan Law Review,
Volume 118, Issue 8; https://repository.law.umich.edu/mlr/vol118/iss8/2/

Although scholars debate just how ineffective CIL is, they almost always
assume that its efficacy depends on its capacity to establish stable
primary rules. And many insist that its efficacy is impaired. 93For
instance, Karol [Wolfke]{.underline} has said that \"the frequently
[expressed doubts about \[CIL\'s\]]{.underline} present
[usefulness]{.underline} . . . seem to be fully justified,\"
[\"\[c\]onsidering \[its\]]{.underline} complexity,
[imprecision]{.underline}, and relative slowness.\" 94Carlos Vásquez has
asserted that [[CIL\'s elasticity presents \"opportunities for
evasion]{.underline}]{.mark} or contestation\" [[that
undercut]{.underline}]{.mark} \[\*1504\] [its
[efficacy]{.mark}]{.underline}. 95Andrew Guzman agrees: [\"\[T\]he lack
of precision in CIL rules does indeed undermine the force of the rules
and generate skepticism about their importance.\"]{.underline} 96

The usual response to these criticisms is, like the ILC\'s, to double
down on the rulebook\--to insist that CIL\'s secondary rules limit its
elasticity and help establish stable primary rules. 97As I explain
below, this response is uncompelling. [[CIL does not]{.mark} actually
[conform to a rulebook]{.mark}]{.underline}. Thus, [as long as the
rulebook conception is the metric for evaluating it, CIL will come up
short]{.underline}. But what if the rulebook conception is misinformed?
What if the problem lies not with CIL but with the conceptual apparatus
that we have been using to assess it?

III\. A PRACTICE-ORIENTED ALTERNATIVE

It\'s hard to overstate just how dissociated the rulebook conception is
from CIL\'s day-to-day operation. As discussed, [[the process]{.mark}
for CIL [is]{.mark} extremely [messy and]{.mark} produces a lot of
[disconnected]{.mark} raw data]{.underline}. In the rulebook conception,
this process can create CIL only if the output satisfies certain
secondary rules and manifests as primary rules. The ILC Conclusions say
that, for this to happen, the mix of practice and opinio juris must be
sufficiently clear and consistent. Otherwise, the output cannot be CIL.

That depiction of CIL is inaccurate. It does not describe the normative
material that global actors in the ordinary course recognize and use as
CIL. This material neither derives from secondary rules nor manifests
only as primary rules. The reason why is that the disorderly process for
CIL largely determines what it is. 98Because this process is so messy,
the normative material that it produces does not come only, or even
primarily, in the form of rules. [[It]{.mark} often [is contingent and
variable]{.mark}]{.underline}. Put differently, although the CIL process
sometimes produces norms that have the clarity and stability of rules,
[most of its normative [output is]{.mark} more
[fragmentary]{.mark}\--treated and accepted as CIL by some actors or in
certain settings but not by or in others]{.underline}. This material
cannot be CIL under the rulebook conception. But it routinely shapes how
people understand and interact with CIL. It is in a very real sense CIL.

\[\*1505\] To be clear, I am here presenting an alternative account of
CIL that describes what it is as a real-world sociological phenomenon.
Let me address at the outset one likely objection. Some will say that
CIL, as I am describing it, is too unmoored or inconstant to be law. A
prominent family of jurisprudential theories posits that law is by
definition rule-like\--that in order for a social practice to constitute
law, it must in key ways conform to a rulebook. 99The objection would be
that CIL must satisfy the rulebook conception in order to be law.

However, the jurisprudential theories that define law in rule-like terms
are not the only available ones; others are compatible with my account.
100Moreover, the idea that all law must conform to the same universal
criteria, no matter its social context, is itself suspect. People in
this world understand law in all sorts of ways. My goal is not to
resolve what law is in some universal sense but rather to describe a
particular kind of law. If the material that global actors routinely
accept and treat as law does not satisfy a certain jurisprudential
theory, the logical conclusion is not that the material is not law but
that the theory is incomplete; it is detached from and an inadequate
barometer of what law \"is\" in the real world. After all, law is not
just an abstract theoretical proposition. It is a social phenomenon. To
understand how CIL functions as law, we need to move beyond those
theories\--as I do here.

A. The Practice of Customary International Law

[CIL\'s defining [characteristics are not clarity]{.mark} or consistency
[but]{.mark} high levels of fluidity, [uncertainty]{.mark}, and
contestation]{.underline}. These characteristics are a function of the
CIL process. Because this process is so unstructured, [[it lacks
mechanisms to regulate]{.underline}]{.mark} which normative positions
are advanced through it or how [[particular
positions]{.underline}]{.mark} play out. Two important points follow.
First, the salience of a position within CIL\--the extent to which the
position is recognized and used as CIL\--does not depend on whether it
satisfies certain secondary rules. What matters instead is how the group
of actors who participate in a given domain of global governance
interact with the position. \[\*1506\] Second, because global actors
take different stances on the normative positions that are batted around
in the CIL process, these positions do not consistently take the form of
conduct rules. They are often inconstant.

**International Law is [broken]{.underline} and not
[effective]{.underline}.**

Linda **Kinstler 25**, writer at the Guardian, 6-26-2025, \"Are we
witnessing the death of international law?\",
https://www.theguardian.com/law/2025/jun/26/are-we-witnessing-the-death-of-international-law

The [[criticisms]{.mark} of [international law]{.mark} are
**familiar**]{.underline} and, in some sense, **[[impossible to
deny]{.underline}]{.mark}**: that it is [[too
**weak**]{.underline}]{.mark}, that it is **[[selectively
applied]{.underline}]{.mark}**, that it is [[merely an
**extension**]{.mark} of **[state power]{.mark}**]{.underline}. "We are
all [[prisoners]{.mark} of this **[horizontal
system]{.mark}**]{.underline}, where states have to keep each other in
check, and that will inevitably fall subject to politicisation," Yusra
Suedi, a lecturer at the University of Manchester, told me. It is
essentially an opt-in system: weak states that flout its directives are
called pariahs or rogues; powerful states that do so are called
hegemons.

For critics of the field, the problem is not just that law doesn't stop
wars or protect civilians, but [also that it [offers]{.mark} a
vocabulary for [states]{.mark} to [justify]{.mark} the **[unchecked use
of]{.mark} [force]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [Breaches are not the
**exception** but the rule]{.underline}. For instance,
[[international]{.mark} humanitarian [law]{.mark} restricts the use of
**certain kinds of weapons**]{.underline}, such as **[cluster
bombs]{.underline}**, that are "[indiscriminate by nature, capable of
**causing harm**]{.underline} without d[istinguishing]{.underline}
between combatants and civilians". [That [did **not stop
Israel**]{.mark}]{.underline}, which first ratified the convention on
certain conventional weapons in 1995, from deploying cluster bombs
against a civilian population in 2006, during the Lebanon war. (Israel,
like the US, Russia, Iran and China, refused to sign the 2008 convention
on cluster munitions.) More than 1,000 people were killed, approximately
one-third of whom were children. The Israeli military claimed that the
use of cluster bombs was not a violation of international law, because
they were focused on military targets and because the population of
Beirut's southern suburbs, an area called Dahiya, had been warned of the
attack in advance.

It was during that conflict that Israeli forces developed what is now
known as the "Dahiya doctrine", which permits the use of
"disproportionate" force against civilian population centres in certain
circumstances. The attack on Dahiya was a blatant violation of what is
known as the "principle of proportionality" in international law, which
holds that civilians may not be attacked if the result is "excessive in
relation to the concrete and direct military advantage anticipated". The
UN human rights council formed a commission to look into the event,
which concluded that "there was no justification" for the use of cluster
bombs.

Every scholar [who **[laments]{.mark}** the [**waning** of
**international law**]{.mark} will have their own moment when the rot
set in]{.underline}. In Koskenniemi's view, it came in the 1960s when
its original goals of peace and justice started to be displaced by the
administrative and managerial [[imperatives]{.mark} of
[globalisation]{.mark}. For the Israeli legal scholar Itamar Mann,
Dahiya felt like the [beginning of the end]{.mark} of international law
[as a credible]{.mark} [system for **preventing
atrocities**]{.mark}]{.underline}. "They weren't just ignoring the rule:
the[y were invoking it for the very purposes that it was [supposed to
**limit** or **control**]{.mark}]{.underline}," he said. By
[[attempting]{.mark} to **[justify]{.mark}** a **[legally]{.mark}
[unjustified action]{.mark}**]{.underline} in the [language of
**international law**]{.underline}, Israel made a mockery of the spirit
and letter of the law. "In general, that was the moment when the idea
that an entire neighbourhood can be eliminated, or close to that, came
on the radar," said Mann. As we spoke about the condition of
international law writ large, he slipped into the past tense: "It was a
way of challenging brute uses of force, and that is no longer there."

### AT: Net-Benefit\-\--Climate

#### No impact to climate change

**Wang 23** \[4/17/23, Seaver Wang works at the Breakthrough Institute
as Director of the Climate and Energy team, "There Is No Climate Tipping
Point," The Breakthrough Institute,
https://thebreakthrough.org/journal/climate-change-banned-words/climate-tipping-point-real\]MZ

It turns out the [future of our climate is a little more
complicated]{.underline} anyways. Many aspects of my unfinished novel's
imagined climate future are very much a part of the research consensus
on climate change---more frequent and intense wildfires, changes in the
water cycle, the decimation of pine forests by invasive beetles. [My
underlying [assumption]{.mark}]{.underline}, however---that at some
specific moment in my lifetime, [the [Earth]{.mark} would [cross]{.mark}
a climate [tipping point]{.mark}]{.underline} and descend into
self-reinforcing madness---[was entirely
[incorrect]{.mark}.]{.underline}

For the set of likely future scenarios we face today, the
[[climate]{.mark} science [literature]{.mark} has [not
identified]{.mark} [any]{.mark} approaching [global tipping
point]{.mark}]{.underline} after which runaway climate change
intensifies beyond humanity's ability to arrest it. [While
[tipping]{.mark} [elements]{.mark}]{.underline} of the climate
system---like Arctic permafrost thaw and loss of Amazon forest
area---[do influence total warming, the [magnitude]{.mark} of this
influence is [substantially smaller]{.mark}]{.underline} than the
societal factors that will ultimately determine the planet's climate
trajectory.

To you, the reader, the lines above may feel stiff and overly qualified.
They, like countless similar sentences in IPCC reports and academic
papers, rely on very careful choice of terminology to maintain their
accuracy. Yet such care seems increasingly incompatible with a climate
discourse that, at times, nurses a weakness for spectacle while trying
to satisfy a hunger for clarity about the future that researchers cannot
yet provide.

One example that holds fascination for me today, just as it did for my
younger novelist self, is the topic of tipping points. More formally
called "climate tipping elements," this subject is a continuously
evolving body of knowledge that has been particularly susceptible to
confusion and misinterpretation.

Over the years, groups of earth scientists have offered slightly
different definitions of tipping elements, and lists of proposed tipping
elements have changed with various additions and removals. Tropical
monsoons and abrupt changes in the El Niño cycle have fallen out of many
lists, while worry over the East Antarctic ice-sheet's stability is
increasing. Meanwhile, research that presents a range of possible
climate impacts after global temperatures cross some theorized tipping
point often gets summarized by writers and journalists using upper-end,
worst-case potential outcomes.

It is not particularly surprising that when arcane scientific detail
meets simplistic reporting, it results in a muddle, and it's a muddle
the term "tipping point" itself---evoking an abruptness and immediacy
not necessarily characteristic of most, let alone all, tipping
elements---perhaps adds to. Climate tipping points and "runaway climate
change" are now common parlance in climate conversations, and while
different advocates may mean different things in practice when using
these terms, they speak to a broadly shared, yet [[inaccurate]{.mark},
[understanding]{.mark} [that]{.mark} the [climate system is]{.mark} on
the verge of very [unstable]{.mark}, self-reinforcing, [and]{.mark}
abruptly [rapid disaster]{.mark}.]{.underline}

It isn't. And [it's important to understand that it isn't]{.underline}.
Taken at rational face value, the [[feeling]{.mark} [that]{.mark} the
[planet is]{.mark} just years away from [sliding beyond a catastrophic
point]{.mark} of no return [invites unproductive
fatalism]{.mark}]{.underline}. At the same time, the [perception of an
imminent climate cliff]{.underline} seemingly rules out long-term
planning in favor of emergency measures, [skewing [discussion]{.mark} of
climate policies in ways that can be
[counterproductive]{.mark}.]{.underline}

### AT: Net Benefit\-\--Food Insecurity 

#### No food wars.

**Vestby '18** \[Vestby, Ida Rudolfsen, and Halvard Buhaug; 5-18-18;
Doctoral Researcher at the Peace Research Institute Oslo; doctoral
researcher at the Department of Peace and Conflict Research at Uppsala
University and PRIO; Research Professor at the Peace Research Institute
Oslo (PRIO); Professor of Political Science at the Norwegian University

Peace Research and Political Geography; "Does hunger cause conflict?"
Prio,
https://blogs.prio.org/ClimateAndConflict/2018/05/does-hunger-cause-conflict/\]

It is perhaps surprising, then, that there is [[little]{.mark} scholarly
[merit]{.mark} in the notion [that]{.mark}]{.underline} a short-term
reduction in access to [[food increases]{.mark} the probability that
[conflict]{.mark} will break out]{.underline}. This is because to start
or participate in [violent conflict requires people to have both the
means and the will. Most people on the brink of starvation are not in
the position to resort to violence]{.underline}, whether against the
government or other social groups. In fact, the urban middle classes
tend to be the most likely to protest against rises in food prices,
since they often have the best opportunities, the most energy, and the
best skills to coordinate and participate in protests.

Accordingly, there is a widespread misapprehension that social unrest in
periods of high food prices relates primarily to food shortages. [In
reality, the sources of [discontent]{.mark} are considerably [more
complex]{.mark} -- [linked to]{.mark} political structures,
[land]{.mark} ownership, [corruption]{.mark}, the desire for [democratic
reforms and]{.mark} general [econ]{.mark}omic problems]{.underline} --
where the price of food is seen in the context of general increases in
the cost of living. [Research has shown]{.underline} that [[while
the]{.mark} international [media]{.mark}]{.underline} have a
[[tend]{.underline}]{.mark}ency [[to seek]{.underline}]{.mark} [simple
[resource]{.mark}-related [explanations]{.mark}]{.underline} -- such as
drought or famine -- for conflicts in the Global South,
[[debates]{.underline}]{.mark} in the local media
[[are]{.underline}]{.mark} permeated by [more
[complex]{.mark}]{.underline} political relationships.

#### Consultation is [normal means]{.underline}.

**Eid \'19** \[Troy A. Eid, \"Consulting With Native American Tribes on

https://www.gtlaw-environmentalandenergy.com/2019/10/articles/compliance/consulting-with-native-american-tribes-on-energy-and-infrastructure-development-strategies-for-reducing-project-risk/\]

"[[Tribal consultation]{.underline}]{.mark}" [[refers to the federal
government's **legal obligation** to consult with Native American tribes
on **energy and infrastructure** projects]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as
highways and railroads, pipelines, telecommunications towers and
systems, and electrical transmission lines. [Whenever a given project
requires some sort of federal approval]{.underline} -- a water-crossing
permit from the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers, for instance, or a
certificate from the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission to build a new
natural gas pipeline -- [the]{.underline} tribal **[consultation
requirement kicks in]{.underline}**.

[The [project **need not be on tribal land** for the]{.mark} tribal
consultation [requirement to apply]{.mark}]{.underline}. On the
contrary, the National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA) and the National
Historic Preservation Act (NHPA), along with many other federal laws,
mandate that [**the lead agency** on each project must consult with all
affected Indian tribes, on a government-to-government
basis]{.underline}. This is [true whether the project is on public or
private land]{.underline}. The rule of thumb is that [[if a project
needs federal permission]{.mark} to proceed, [the]{.mark}]{.underline}
[federal [agency considering it **must** identify]{.mark} the [tribes in
the]{.mark} project [area and consult with them in a **meaningful
fashion**]{.mark}]{.underline} before making any final decisions.
